Let this lesson push you to examine your heart: are you quick to attribute your failures to God's decree rather than your own choices? Resolve today to take full ownership of your actions while still trusting in God's overarching wisdom.
?
The Mu'tazilite objection rests on the assumption that if God creates opposite wills, He must be willing opposites. But the Maturidi response (though incomplete here) likely distinguishes between God's will to create and the object of that creation. In your daily struggles with others whose actions seem opposed to what you believe is right, do you treat them as enemies or as part of God's complex plan? When you encounter someone whose will contradicts yours, can you see them as a created locus of God's will too? How does that shift your response?

Impossibility of God Creating Power for the Impossible
Nasafi argues impossibility of creating power for the impossible via analogy with knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Nasafi argues that it is impossible for God to create power for an act that He Himself cannot perform, using an analogy with knowledge and common sense.

Core Idea
You cannot give what you do not have — power, like knowledge, must be possessed to be bestowed.

This lesson presents a key argument from Imam Nasafi against the Mu’tazilite view that God can create power in a servant for an act that God Himself cannot do. Nasafi appeals to both rational analogy and common sense, showing that even the simplest mind recognizes the absurdity of such a claim. By understanding this argument, you will grasp a fundamental principle in Maturidi theology regarding divine attributes and their relation to creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Notice how the author moves from a general statement to analogy and then to common sense.
2
Identify the core analogy
Nasafi draws a parallel between power and knowledge. Find where he compares creating power from one who is not powerful to creating knowledge from one who is not knowledgeable.
This is the rational backbone of the argument.
3
Examine the appeal to common sense
Nasafi says even the dullest person would find the Mu'tazilite view foolish. Reflect on why he uses this appeal to intuition ('badaha') and primary intellects.
4
Consider the refutation of the Mu'tazila
Understand how this argument undermines the Mu'tazilite position that God can grant power over acts He cannot do. The argument shows it is logically impossible.
This connects to the broader Maturidi defense of divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عنه عاجز وهو محال ، وكذا من المحال أن يعلم ذات غيره شيئًا لم يكن ذلك الشيء معلومًا لهذا العلم من غير فصل بين القديم والمحدث، فكذا هذا، وإحالة هذا مما تعرفه العوام ببدائههم وأوائل عقولهم حتى لو صور لأغبى خليقة الله تعالى من العوام قول المعتزلة في تجويز تخليق الله تعالى القدرة للعبد على ما لا يقدر الله تعالى عليه بنفسه أو يقدر أحد من العباد إقدار ذات ما على ما لا يقدر عليه الذات المقدر، لتسارعوا إلى تسفيه القائل له الراضى بذلك مذهبًا لنفسه، كما يعرفون إحالة تعليم ذات ما ذاتاً ما لا علم لا للمعلم به، ويتسارعون إلى تسفيه المجوز لذلك وذلك محال، وخروج عن قضية البداهة وأوائل العقول فكذا هذا، ألا ترى أن القول بتخليق قدرة ممن ليس بقادر عليها محال كما أن القول بتخليق علم ممن ليس بعالم به محال فكذا القول بتخليق قدرة على فعل ممن ليس بقادر على ذلك الفعل يكون محالاً كالقول بتخليق علم بمعلوم ممن ليس بعالم بذلك المعلوم.
Unable to do it, and that is impossible. And likewise, it is impossible that a being should know something of another being that was not known to this knowledge without distinction between the eternal and the originated, so likewise this. And the impossibility of this is known by the common people through their innate dispositions and primary intellects, such that if the statement of the Mu'tazila were presented to the dullest of God's creatures among the common people—their permitting God to create power for the servant over what God Himself is not able to do, or for one of the servants to empower a being over what that being is not able to do—they would hasten to declare foolish the one who says this and is satisfied with it as a doctrine for himself, just as they know the impossibility of a being teaching another being something that the teacher has no knowledge of, and they hasten to declare foolish the one who permits that. And that is impossible, and a departure from the dictate of intuition and primary intellects. So likewise this. Do you not see that the statement of creating power from one who is not powerful over it is impossible, just as the statement of creating knowledge from one who is not knowledgeable of it is impossible? So likewise the statement of creating power for an act from one who is not powerful over that act is impossible, like the statement of creating knowledge of a known thing from one who is not knowledgeable of that known thing.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This is a proof that it is impossible for God to create power in a creature for an act that God Himself cannot perform. The author begins by stating the impossibility directly: a being cannot be unable to do something and then create that ability in another. He then draws an analogy with knowledge: just as it is impossible for a being to teach something it does not know, so it is impossible for God to grant power over an act He cannot do. The author appeals to common sense, arguing that even the most ignorant person would recognize the absurdity of such a claim if presented with the Mu'tazilite view. The proof concludes that creating power from one who is not powerful over that act is as impossible as creating knowledge from one who is not knowledgeable of that known thing.

Key Terms
قدرة
تخليق
بداهة
أوائل العقول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the analogy between power and knowledge means they are identical in every way.
Correction: Nasafi uses the analogy only to illustrate a common principle: the giver must possess the attribute to bestow it. The analogy does not imply that power and knowledge are the same attribute.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the basis for the impossibility of God creating power for an act He cannot do?
A) The Mu'tazilite doctrine of free will.
B) An analogy with knowledge: a being cannot teach what it does not know.
C) The fact that common people cannot understand such a concept.
D) The distinction between eternal and originated beings.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi proves that God cannot grant power over an act He cannot do, because the source must possess the attribute to bestow it — just as one cannot teach what one does not know.

?
Nasafi appeals to innate intuition (fitrah) as sufficient to reject false claims. Have you ever silenced your own inner sense of truth to accept a theological idea that felt wrong? What step can you take today to better trust the fitrah that Allah has placed in you to discern truth?

Refuting the Analogy Between Creating Will and Creating Power
Nasafi responds to the Mu'tazilite analogy by denying its premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazilite analogy between creating will and creating power fails according to Nasafi.

Core Idea
God's universal will breaks the analogy.

The Mu'tazilites argued that if God can create will without willing, then He can create power without being powerful. Nasafi responds by asserting that God's will is universal, so the analogy is invalid. This lesson examines the objection and its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the Mu'tazilite claim: since it's permissible for God to create will for an act without being willing, why can't He create power without being powerful?
The objection is based on a presumed symmetry between will and power.
2
Identify Nasafi's key move
Look at the response. Nasafi says there is no difference between the two cases: just as it's impossible for God to create power for an act He cannot do, it's impossible for Him to create will for an act He does not will.
He is denying the premise that creating will without willing is permissible.
3
Understand the underlying principle
Nasafi mentions the doctrine of the universality of God's will (عموم الإرادة), which holds that God's will encompasses all originated things. Therefore, He cannot create will for something He does not will.
This principle is what makes the analogy fail.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: لما جاز أن يخلق الباري جل جلاله إرادة الفعل لا يكون هذا مريدًا له، فلم لا يجوز أن يخلق قدرة على فعل لا قدرة له عليه؟ قيل: عندنا لا فرق بين هذا وبين ما ألزمنا، فإن الله تعالى كما يستحيل أن يخلق قدرة لغيره على فعل لا قدرة له على ذلك الفعل يستحيل أن يخلق إرادة لغيره لفعل لا يكون هو مريدًا لذلك الفعل لما نبين بعد هذا أن من مذهبنا القول بعموم إرادة الله تعالى المحدثات، وهذا السؤال بنيتم على أصلكم الفاسد.
They said: Since it is permissible for the Exalted Creator to create will for an act without being willing it, why is it not permissible for Him to create power for an act without having power over it? It was said: According to us, there is no difference between this and what we have argued. For God, just as it is impossible for Him to create power for another over an act over which He has no power, similarly it is impossible for Him to create will for another for an act for which He is not willing. This is because we will later explain that our doctrine holds the universality of God's will over originated things. And this objection is built upon your corrupt principle.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage records an objection from the Mu'tazilites and the author's answer. The objectors argue that if God can create will in a creature without Himself willing that act, then He should also be able to create power in a creature without Himself being powerful over that act. The answer denies the premise: according to the author's school, it is equally impossible for God to create will for an act He does not will, because God's will is universal and encompasses all originated things. Therefore, the analogy fails, and the objection is based on the opponents' corrupt principle (i.e., that God's will is not universal).

Misconception: Creating will and creating power are analogous; if God can create will without willing, He can create power without being powerful.
Correction: The analogy fails because God's will is universal; He cannot create will for an act He does not will, just as He cannot create power for an act He cannot do.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite objection is refuted by the doctrine of the universality of God's will.

Contradictory Wills Objection and Distinction Between Will and Wish
Nasafi answers the objection of contradictory wills by distinguishing real will from mere wish.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi defends God's universal will against the objection of contradictory wills by distinguishing genuine will from mere wish.

Core Idea
Real will must be accompanied by its act; otherwise it is only wish.

The objectors claim that if God wills all things, He would have to will contradictory acts in the same place and time, which is impossible. Nasafi responds by clarifying that true will is inseparable from its corresponding act. If an act does not occur, what was created in the creature is not will but wish. This distinction resolves the apparent conflict while preserving God's universal will.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the objectors' argument that universal will leads to impossible contradictions. Notice how they assume that God creates wills in creatures.
The objection is presented as a logical consequence of the view that God is the creator of all wills.
2
Identify Nasafi's initial response
Notice how he characterizes the objection as either sophistry or ignorance of the true nature of will.
This is a rhetorical move before the substantive answer.
3
Understand the key distinction
The author distinguishes real will from wish based on whether the act accompanies it. Real will necessarily coincides with its act; otherwise it is mere wish.
This distinction is the core of the answer.
4
Apply the distinction to the objection
Realize that if an act does not occur, the corresponding intention in the creature is not will but wish. Thus no contradiction arises because God does not create two real wills for contradictory acts.
This resolves the objection.
5
Note the promise of further explanation
The author promises to clarify this distinction further in the later section on will.
This shows the systematic nature of the work.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: القول بما قلتم يؤدي إلى أن يوجد من الله تعالى إرادة للمتضادين في محل واحد في وقت واحد، فإنه إذا خلق لذات ما إرادة لفعل في محل، وخلق لآخر إرادة ضد ذلك الفعل في عين ذلك المحل في تلك الساعة، وكان هو تعالى مريدًا ما خلق لكل واحد منهما إرادته من الفعل كان مؤديا إلى ذلك وهو محال. قيل: هذا السؤال تمويه منكم إن عرفتم حقيقة الإرادة وإلا فهو صادر عن الجهل بحقيقتها، وهذا لأن تخليق الإرادة لذاتين لفعلين متضادين في وقت واحد في محل واحد محال كتخليق فعلين، فإن الإرادة بدون الفعل محال وكل ما يظن إرادة إذا لم يطابقه الفعل ولم يقترن به فهو تمن وليس بإرادة فلا يكون كل واحد منهما إرادة بل ما قارنه الفعل فهو إرادة لم يقارنه الفعل فهو تمن على ما نبين حقيقة هذا إذا انتهينا إلى مسألة الإرادة - إن شاء الله تعالى.
They said: The statement you have made leads to the existence from God of will for two contradictory things in one place at one time. For if He creates for a being a will for an act in a place, and creates for another a will for the opposite of that act in the very same place at that same moment, and He Himself is willing what He created for each of them of the act, then this leads to that, and it is impossible. It was said: This objection is a sophistry from you if you know the reality of will; otherwise it arises from ignorance of its reality. This is because creating will for two beings for two contradictory acts at one time in one place is impossible, like creating two acts. For will without act is impossible, and everything that is thought to be will if the act does not correspond to it and is not connected with it is wish and not will. Thus each of them is not will; rather, that which the act accompanies is will, and that which the act does not accompany is wish. We will explain the reality of this when we reach the question of will, God willing.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection and its answer. The objectors argue that if God's will is universal, then He would have to will contradictory acts in the same place and time, which is impossible. The author's answer is twofold. First, he accuses the objectors of either sophistry or ignorance of the true nature of will. Second, he explains that real will is necessarily accompanied by its corresponding act; otherwise, it is not will but mere wish (tamannī). Therefore, if God creates a will for an act that does not occur, that is not will but wish. Since God cannot will contradictions (as that would require both acts to occur), He does not create real will for both. The author promises to clarify this further in the section on will.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God could will contradictory things if He creates wills in creatures—for example, willing both an act and its opposite.
Correction: The author shows that real will requires the act to happen, so God only wills what actually occurs. If an act does not occur, the corresponding intention is wish, not will.

Key Takeaway
Real will is defined by its conjunction with the act; without the act, it is only wish, and thus God cannot be said to will contradictory acts.

Distinguishing Wish from Will to Refute the Objection
Nasafi distinguishes wish from will to refute the objection about creating wish without being the wisher.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi distinguishes wish (tamannī) from will (irādah) to refute the objection that God creating power without being powerful is analogous to creating wish without being a wisher.

Core Idea
Wish and will are distinct; wish involves absence of the act, while will implies its existence.

In this passage, Nasafi addresses a third objection against the doctrine that God cannot create power in a creature without Himself possessing that power. The objectors draw an analogy to wish, but Nasafi shows that wish is fundamentally different from will, and this difference refutes the analogy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the overall argument, then read the translation. Pay attention to the objection and the distinction introduced.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the objection
The objectors argue: since God can create wish in a creature without being a wisher, He should be able to create power without being powerful. Note how this analogy is set up.
3
Understand the distinction between wish and will
Nasafi explains that wish (tamannī) is defined by the absence of the act, whereas will (irādah) requires the act to exist. This is the key difference that breaks the analogy.
The term 'مفارقة الفعل' (separation of the act) is crucial.
4
See how the distinction refutes the objection
Since God's will is always effective, He can never wish for something that does not occur. Therefore, creating wish in a creature does not imply that God is a wisher, but creating power would require God to have that power Himself.
The refutation hinges on the different implications of wish vs. power.
5
Reflect on the theological implication
Consider why this distinction matters for understanding God's attributes: wish is not an attribute of God, but power is. This protects the doctrine that God's attributes are unique and not transferable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: لما جاز أن يخلق الله التمني لفعل لا يكون متمنيًا لذلك الفعل لماذا لا يجوز أن يخلق قدرة على فعل لا يكون هو قادرا على ذلك الفعل؟ قيل: هذا السؤال فاسد لما مر أن التمني يمتاز عن الإرادة بوجود مفارقة الفعل إياه وعدم المفارقة، ولا يجوز أن لا يوجد ما يريده الله تعالى فلا يتصور منه التمني، فإن كان ذلك الفعل مما يوجد فما وجد من العبد إرادة وتعلقت به أيضا إرادة الله تعالى وإن كان ذلك الفعل مما لا يوجد فما حصل من العبد تمن لا إرادة والله تعالى كان مريدًا عدم ذلك الفعل لا وجوده فبعدم ذلك الفعل الذي يريد عدمه لا يكون متمنيًا بل يكون مريدًا عدمه، ألا ترى أنه لا يكون متمنيًا للتمني الذي خلقه فلا يكون متمنيًا للفعل الذي خلق له التمني، فأما فيما نحن فيه فيستحيل أن يخلق قدرة لا قدرة له عليها، فيستحيل أن يخلق قدرة على فعل لا قدرة له عليه، وفي التمني بخلافه والله الموفق.
They said: Since it is permissible for God to create wish for an act without being a wisher of that act, why is it not permissible for Him to create power for an act without being powerful over that act? It was said: This question is corrupt because it has already been mentioned that wish is distinguished from will by the existence of the act's separation from it and the lack of separation. And it is not permissible that what God wills should not exist, so wish is inconceivable from Him. For if that act is of the kind that exists, then what exists from the servant is will, and God's will is also attached to it. And if that act is of the kind that does not exist, then what occurs from the servant is wish, not will, and God is willing the non-existence of that act, not its existence. So by the non-existence of that act which He wills not to exist, He is not a wisher; rather, He is willing its non-existence. Do you not see that He is not a wisher of the wish He creates, so He is not a wisher of the act for which He created the wish? As for what we are discussing, it is impossible for Him to create power over which He has no power, so it is impossible for Him to create power for an act over which He has no power. And in the case of wish, it is different. And God is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage contains a third objection and its answer. The objectors argue: if God can create wish in a creature without Himself wishing that act, then He should be able to create power without being powerful. The answer explains that wish is fundamentally different from will. Wish is defined by the absence of the act, while will requires the act to occur. God's will is always fulfilled; what He wills must exist. Therefore, God never wishes (tamannī) because that would imply He wills something that does not happen. If an act exists, the creature's inclination is will, not wish, and God also wills it. If an act does not exist, the creature's inclination is wish, but God wills its non-existence. Thus, God is not a wisher for the act He creates wish for. The impossibility of creating power without power remains, but wish is a different case because it does not imply the attribute in the creator.

إرادة
مفارقة الفعل
قدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Wish and will are essentially the same thing; if God can create wish without being a wisher, He can also create power without being powerful.
Correction: Wish is fundamentally different from will because wish involves the absence of the act, while will requires its existence. God's will is always fulfilled, so He never wishes. Therefore, the analogy with power is invalid.

Checkpoint

C) Because God never creates wish, only will.
D) Because power is an attribute of God, but wish is not.

Key Takeaway
Wish and will are distinct: wish involves the absence of the act, so creating wish does not imply the attribute in the creator, whereas power does, thus the analogy fails.

Distinguishing Divine Will from Wish
Refutes an objection by distinguishing will from wish.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between divine will (irada) and wish (tamanni), and why this distinction is crucial for refuting objections about God's power and creation.

Core Idea
Will must be accompanied by its act; otherwise it is mere wish.

This passage presents a theological objection against the Maturidi position on divine will and power. The objector argues that if God can create a will without His own corresponding act, a contradiction follows. Imam al-Nasafi responds by drawing a sharp line between genuine will and wish, showing that what appears to be will without act is actually wish. This distinction preserves God's omnipotence and avoids attributing imperfection to Him.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objector claims that if God creates will for two agents for opposing acts at the same time and place, it leads to impossibility. Notice the structure of the objection.
The objection assumes that both created wills are genuine wills.
2
Identify the response's key point
The author distinguishes will from wish: will always accompanies its act; if not, it is wish. This dissolves the alleged contradiction because what the objector calls 'will' is actually wish.
The response defines will essentially as act-accompanying.
3
Examine the second objection about power
The objector draws an analogy: if God can create wish without being a wisher, why can't He create power without being powerful? Read this carefully.
4
Understand the resolution
The author rejects the analogy by showing that power necessarily implies the possessor's ability, while wish does not. God cannot create a power He does not possess, but He can create a wish He does not entertain.
This upholds God's absolute power over all acts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مريدًا ما خلق لكل واحد منهما إرادته من الفعل كان مؤديا إلى ذلك وهو محال.
قيل: هذا السؤال تمويه منكم إن عرفتم حقيقة الإرادة وإلا فهو صادر عن الجهل بحقيقتها، وهذا لأن تخليق الإرادة لذاتين لفعلين متضادين في وقت واحد في محل واحد محال كتخليق فعلين، فإن الإرادة بدون الفعل محال وكل ما يظن إرادة إذا لم يطابقه الفعل ولم يقترن به فهو تمن وليس بإرادة فلا يكون كل واحد منهما إرادة بل ما قارنه الفعل فهو إرادة لم يقارنه الفعل فهو تمن على ما نبين حقيقة هذا إذا انتهينا إلى مسألة الإرادة - إن شاء الله تعالى.
فإن قالوا: لما جاز أن يخلق الله التمني لفعل لا يكون متمنيًا لذلك الفعل لماذا لا يجوز أن يخلق قدرة على فعل لا يكون هو قادرا على ذلك الفعل؟ قيل: هذا السؤال فاسد لما مر أن التمني يمتاز عن الإرادة بوجود مفارقة الفعل إياه وعدم المفارقة، ولا يجوز أن لا يوجد ما يريده الله تعالى فلا يتصور منه التمني، فإن كان ذلك الفعل مما يوجد فما وجد من العبد إرادة وتعلقت به أيضا إرادة الله تعالى وإن كان ذلك الفعل مما لا يوجد فما حصل من العبد تمن لا إرادة والله تعالى كان مريدًا عدم ذلك الفعل لا وجوده فبعدم ذلك الفعل الذي يريد عدمه لا يكون متمنيًا بل يكون مريدًا عدمه، ألا ترى أنه لا يكون متمنيًا للتمني الذي خلقه فلا يكون متمنيًا للفعل الذي خلق له التمني، فأما فيما نحن فيه فيستحيل أن يخلق قدرة لا قدرة له عليها، فيستحيل أن يخلق قدرة على فعل لا قدرة له عليه، وفي التمني بخلافه والله الموفق.
Wishing to create for each of them His will from the act would lead to that, and it is impossible. It was said: This question is a deception from you if you know the reality of will, otherwise it arises from ignorance of its reality. This is because creating will for two essences for two opposing acts at one time in one place is impossible, like creating two acts. For will without the act is impossible, and everything that is thought to be will if the act does not correspond to it and is not conjoined with it is a wish and not a will. So neither of them is a will, but rather that which the act accompanies is a will; that which the act does not accompany is a wish, as we will explain the reality of this when we come to the question of will, God willing. If they say: Since it is permissible for God to create wish for an act without Him being a wisher for that act, why is it not permissible for Him to create power over an act without Him being powerful over that act? It was said: This question is corrupt because it has passed that wish is distinguished from will by the existence of the act's separation from it and non-separation. And it is not permissible that what God wills should not exist, so wish cannot be conceived from Him. If that act is something that exists, then what exists from the servant is will, and the will of God is also attached to it. And if that act is something that does not exist, then what occurs from the servant is wish, not will, and God is the one willing the non-existence of that act, not its existence. By the non-existence of that act which He wills not to exist, He is not a wisher, but rather He is a willer of its non-existence. Do you not see that He is not a wisher for the wish which He created, so He is not a wisher for the act for which He created the wish? As for what we are discussing, it is impossible for Him to create a power over which He has no power, so it is impossible for Him to create power over an act over which He has no power. And in the case of wish it is otherwise. God is the giver of success.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer argument about divine will and power. The author first presents a hypothetical: if God were to create a will for each of two agents (Himself and the servant) for opposing acts, that would lead to contradiction and is impossible. Then he responds by distinguishing true will (irada) from wish (tamanni). He explains that creating will for two agents for opposing acts at the same time in one place is impossible, just like creating two acts, because will without the corresponding act is impossible. What is mistakenly thought to be will but is not accompanied by the act is actually wish, not will. Therefore, in the objection, neither of the created wills would be genuine will; only the one accompanied by the act is will, the other is wish. The objector then raises a parallel: if God can create wish for an act without Himself wishing that act, why can He not create power over an act without Himself being powerful over it? The author answers that this analogy is invalid because wish differs from will in that the act may or may not accompany it, whereas power is directly tied to ability. God cannot create a power that He does not possess. If the act in question exists, then both the servant's will and God's will attach to it. If the act does not exist, the servant's state is wish, not will, while God wills its non-existence. Thus God is never a mere wisher for what He wills to exist. Consequently, it is impossible for God to create a power over an act without Himself having power over that act, unlike wish where the act may not accompany it. The conclusion underscores the distinction between divine power and human power, preserving God's omnipotence.

Misconception: Some may think that God creating a will without the act is similar to creating a wish without the act.
Correction: The author clarifies that will necessarily entails the act, while wish does not. Thus God never creates a will without the corresponding act; what appears as such is actually wish.

One Act Under Two Agents: The Impossibility of Shared Power
Dialectical argument against shared divine-human power over one act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why affirming that both God and the servant have power over the same act leads to disabling God, which is impossible.

Core Idea
If two agents fully power the same act, God is disabled.

In this passage, the author tackles a divisive theological issue: can a single act be under the power of both God and the human servant? The argument explores the logical consequences of such a claim, ultimately showing that it leads to either absurdity or disabling God. Let's follow the dialectic carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation without analyzing. Get a feel for the argument's flow.
The passage is dense with dialectical moves. Pay attention to the conditional statements.
2
Identify the two possibilities
The author sets up two possibilities: either both agents share power over the same act (first possibility) or each uniquely brings it into existence (second possibility). The first is dismissed quickly, so focus on the second.
The second possibility is attributed to a certain group.
3
Follow the dilemma against the second possibility
The author poses a dilemma: if the servant brings the act into existence, does God retain power to bring it into existence? If yes, that's creating what already exists (absurd). If no, then the servant removes God's power (disabling God). Both are impossible.
This is the core of the argument.
4
Consider the objector's response and its refutation
The objector suggests God could bring the act into existence in a second state. The author refutes this by showing that if the act is transitory, making it exist again would imply it remains, contradicting its nature. If it endures, then it already exists, so re-creation is impossible.
The refutation uses the concept of 'remaining' (بقاء) to expose a contradiction.
5
Conclude the lesson
Recognize that the entire argument demonstrates that affirming creative power for the servant inevitably leads to the impossible conclusion of disabling God. Thus, such a claim is false.
This reinforces the Islamic doctrine that only God is the true creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

ثم على هذا القسم نلزم فنقول : إذا أوجد العبد هل بقى الله تعالى على ذلك الفعل قدرة الإيجاد ؟ فإن قالوا: نعم، فقد أحالوا ، لأن إيجاد الموجود محل. وإن قالوا: لا، فقد أقروا بإزالة العبد قدرة الله تعالى عما كانت ثابتة عليه ومنعه إياه عن إيجاد ما كان قادرا على إيجاده، وهذا هو التعجيز عندنا والمنع عندكم، وكل ذلك محال ممتنع، والله الموفق.

And this section is fitting for the third chapter, and it is that one object of power falling under the power of two powerful agents is among the possibilities. We only paused on it in this act due to the necessity of division. And if this section is invalidated, the other section is determined, namely, that God has power over it just as the servant has power over it, and each one is unique in bringing it into existence, as a group of them held. Then on this section we impose a necessary consequence and say: If the servant brings it into existence, does God remain with power of bringing it into existence over that act? If they say: Yes, then they have committed an impossibility, because bringing into existence what already exists is absurd. If they say: No, then they have admitted that the servant removes God's power from what was established over it and prevents Him from bringing into existence what He was able to bring into existence. This is disabling according to us and prevention according to you, and all of that is impossible and impermissible. God is the giver of success. The servant. If they say: God brings it into existence in the second state from the state of its existence which He brought into existence. It was said: The necessary consequence in the first state remains, and you have no objection to it. Then, bringing it into existence in the second state is also impossible. For what the servant brought into existence, in our view, is impossible of remaining, and in your view, with differences among you, it may be something that remains or something that does not remain. If it is something that does not remain, then bringing it into existence in the second state is impossible, because it would become remaining due to its existence in two consecutive times between which non-existence is not conceivable, and this is the definition of remaining, and the statement that what does not remain can remain is impossible. And if it is something that remains, then it is existent in the second time of its existence, and bringing into existence what already exists is impossible. And it is known through these sections that the statement that affirms the power of creation for the servant leads to disabling God, and it is impossible.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical argument concerning whether one object of power can fall under the power of two agents (God and the human servant). The author first states that this is a possible scenario in general, but due to the division of arguments, they consider two possibilities. The first possibility (that both share power on the same act) is invalidated, so the second possibility is determined: God has power over the act just as the servant does, and each one uniquely brings it into existence. This is the position of a certain group. The author then refutes this second possibility by imposing a necessary consequence: if the servant brings the act into existence, does God retain the power to bring it into existence? If they answer 'yes', then that means bringing into existence something already existing, which is absurd. If they answer 'no', then they admit that the servant removes God's power and prevents Him from creating what He was able to create – a form of disabling God. Both options are impossible. The objector then tries to argue that God brings the act into existence in a second state, after the servant's act. The author replies that the first state's consequence remains, and then shows that a second creation is also impossible. If the servant's act is transitory (non-enduring), then creating it again would make it enduring across two consecutive times without an intervening non-existence, which is the definition of remaining – but the objector says it does not remain, so this is contradictory. If the act is enduring, then it already exists in the second time, so creating it again is re-creating what already exists, which is impossible. The conclusion is that affirming creative power for the servant inevitably leads to the impossibility of disabling God, so such a claim is false.

B) It would imply that God is disabled.

Key Takeaway
Affirming that the servant has creative power over an act inevitably leads to the impossibility of disabling God, proving that only Allah is the true creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

How does this realization affect your repentance? ?
The argument in this lesson is not just abstract theology; it exposes a hidden shirk of power. When you worry about your future actions, do you fear your own failure or Allah's decree? Mu`ammar's Claim: Bodies Create Accidents
Nasafi exposes Mu`ammar's view and its polytheistic implication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Mu`ammar's view that bodies, not God, create accidents, and why Nasafi says this implies two creators.

Core Idea
If bodies create accidents, then God is not the only creator.

Nasafi begins by stating the dangerous conclusion of Mu`ammar's position: it leads to polytheism. He then explains Mu`ammar's view that God creates only substances, while bodies produce their own accidents by nature or choice. This sets up the refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp Mu`ammar's core claim and Nasafi's immediate inference.
Notice the phrase 'صانعين' (two creators) appears right at the start.
2
Identify the two parts of creation
Mu`ammar divides the world into substances (God's work) and accidents (bodies' work). Find where this division is stated.
3
Understand the consequence
If accidents are not created by God, then He is not the sole creator. Nasafi calls this 'leading to two creators'.
This is the foundational point for the rest of the refutation.

And we only say: that it leads to the assertion of two creators, because the world is accidents and substances, and God Almighty is the Creator of substances. As for accidents, according to the saying of Mu`ammar, the head of the Mu`tazila: God Almighty has no power over anything of accidents, and He did not undertake their creation nor their existence, nor are they within His power. Rather, bodies themselves create accidents, some by their natures and some by choice. So according to him, God Almighty did not create health nor sickness, nor death nor life, nor color nor smell nor taste, nor form nor composition nor arrangement, nor motion nor stillness, nor other than that. And in this is corruption from several aspects: First: that he permitted the creation of accidents from inanimate objects and lifeless things, which have no life, power, or knowledge, while those accidents are precise, perfected acts, and permitting their creation from one who has no knowledge, power, or life nullifies the Creator being described with these attributes, because the precise, perfected act is evidence for the affirmation of these attributes, and the intellects necessarily judge it to be ignorance and foolishness where it permits precise acts from one with no life, knowledge, or power. Second: that he made partners for God Almighty in the creation of the world, because since the world was divided and only one part came from Him, and the other part came from another, the world was created by Him and by others. Then his saying is worse than the saying of the Magians in several ways: First: that the Magians only affirm one partner for the Creator, while he affirmed countless partners. Second: that he permitted the existence of precise, perfected acts from one with no life, knowledge, or power, and the Magians denied that. And he affirmed for God Almighty partners that are not living, not knowing, not powerful, while the Magians did not permit that. Third: that he made the making of every inanimate and lifeless thing greater than the making of God Almighty, because God Almighty created only bodies, then each body He creates creates — either by its choice or by nature — countless accidents, so corresponding to each individual body of the world whose creation God undertook there are countless things that God does not create. Fourth: that the Magians only attributed to other than the wise Creator evils and ugly things to exalt Him, while he — just as he attributed evils and ugly things like death, illness, disease, chronic ailment, blindness, deafness, lameness, and paralysis to other than God Almighty — also attributed good things and virtues like joy, pleasure, delight, life, health, sight, and hearing to other than God Almighty. So he matched the Magians in attributing evils to other than God, and surpassed them in attributing good things and virtues to other than God. Then knowledge of the temporal origination of bodies is obtained by knowing the temporal origination of accidents, and accidents are not created by God, so God Almighty was not the one who established the evidence for the world's temporal origination nor was He able to establish it. Rather, that was established by another, namely bodies. And similarly, the prophet did not establish the proof of truthfulness, since...
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu`tazilite Mu`ammar's position on the origin of accidents. Nasafi begins by stating that Mu`ammar's view necessarily leads to the belief in two creators. He explains that the world consists of substances (a`yan) and accidents (a`rad). According to Mu`ammar, God creates only substances, while accidents are created by the bodies themselves—some by their nature (tab`), others by choice (ikhtiyar). Thus, God has no power over accidents, and did not create any of them. Nasafi then lists several corrupt consequences of this view. The first aspect: Mu`ammar allows precise, perfected acts (af`al muhkama mutqana) to be produced by lifeless objects (mawat, jumadat) that have no life, power, or knowledge. This contradicts the evidence that such acts require a creator with life, power, and knowledge. If such acts can come from the lifeless, then the Creator's attributes of life, power, and knowledge become meaningless. The intellect clearly rejects this as ignorance and foolishness. The second aspect: Mu`ammar makes partners for God in creation. Since the world is divided into parts created by God (substances) and parts created by others (accidents), God is not the sole creator. Then Nasafi compares this to the Magians (Zoroastrians) and shows Mu`ammar's view is worse in four ways. First, the Magians only affirm one partner for the creator, while Mu`ammar affirms countless partners (since every body creates countless accidents). Second, Mu`ammar permits perfect acts from non-living, ignorant, powerless things, while the Magians deny that. Third, Mu`ammar makes the creation of lifeless things exceed God's own creation: God only creates bodies, but each body creates countless accidents, so the total of what bodies create is greater than what God creates. Fourth, the Magians only attribute evil and ugly things to another principle to exalt God, but Mu`ammar attributes both evil and good to other than God, thus exceeding the Magians in associating partners with God in goodness. Finally, Nasafi points out a theological problem: knowledge of the origin of bodies depends on knowledge of accidents. But if accidents are not created by God, then God does not establish the proof for the origin of the world; rather, bodies do. Similarly, the prophet's proof of truthfulness would not be established by God but by others. The text ends abruptly, but the argument is clear.

أعراض
أعيان
طباع

Correction: Mu`ammar's view is not just a limitation of divine power; it makes bodies creators, leading to polytheism.

Make a firm resolution to consciously attribute every accident of your day to Allah's immediate creation.

First Corruption: Perfect Acts from Lifeless Things
Nasafi presents and analyzes the first corruption of Mu`ammar's view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Nasafi argues that Mu`ammar's view that lifeless bodies produce perfect acts undermines God's attributes.

Core Idea
Lifeless bodies cannot produce perfect, orderly acts.

Nasafi now lists the corruptions of Mu`ammar's view. The first is that he allows precise, perfected acts (af`al muhkama mutqana) to be produced by inanimate objects that have no life, power, or knowledge. This contradicts the evidence that such acts require a wise, powerful creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation for the first corruption.
Notice the phrase 'أفعال محكمة متقنة' (precise perfected acts).
2
Identify the contradiction
The corruption is that Mu`ammar permits perfect acts from lifeless things, which should require life, knowledge, and power.
3
Understand the implication
If lifeless things can produce perfect acts, then the Creator's attributes become meaningless. The intellect rejects this as foolish.
Nasafi says: 'يَبْطُلُ كَوْنُ الصَّانِعِ مَوْصُوفًا بِهَذِهِ الصِّفَاتِ'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما قلنا: إنه يؤدي إلى القول بصانعين، وذلك لأن العالم أعراض وأعيان والله تعالى خالق الأعيان، فأما الأعراض فعلى قول معمر رئيس المعتزلة: لا قدرة الله تعالى على شيء من الأعراض وما تولى هو خلقها ولا إيجادها ولا داخلة تحت قدرته، إنما الأجسام هي التي تخلق الأعراض بعضها بطباعها وبعضها بالاختيار، فما خلق الله تعالى عنده لا صحة ولا سقما ولا موتا ولا حياة ولا لونا ولا رائحة ولا طعما ولا هيئة ولا تركيبا ولا تأليفا ولا حركة ولا سكونا، وغير ذلك. وفيه فساد من وجوه: أحدها: أنه جوز تخليق الأعراض من الموات والجمادات ولا حياة لها ولا قدرة ولا علم، وتلك الأعراض أفعال محكمة متقنة وتجويز تخليقها ممن لا علم له ولا قدرة ولا حياة يبطل كون الصانع موصوفا بهذه الصفات، إذ الفعل المحكم المتقن دليل ثبوت هذه الصفات، ولا بداهة للعقول تحكم عليه بالجهل والحمق حيث جوز الأفعال المحكمة ممن لا حياة له ولا علم ولا قدرة. والآخر أنه جعل الله تعالى شركاء في تخليق العالم إذ العالم لما كان منقسما ولم يكن منه إلا قسم واحد، وكان القسم الآخر حاصلا بغيره كان العالم مخلوقا له ولغيره، ثم كان قوله شرًا من قول المجوس بوجوه: أحدها: أن المجوس لا تثبت للصانع إلا شريكًا واحدًا، وهو أثبت ما لا يحصى من الشركاء. والثاني: أنه جوز وجود الفعل المحكم المتقن ممن لا حياة له ولا علم ولا قدرة، والمجوس أنكروا ذلك. وهو أثبت الله تعالى شركاء غير أحياء لا علماء ولا قادرين والمجوس لم يجوزوا ذلك. والثالث: أنه جعل صنع كل جماد وموات وأكثر من صنع الله تعالى، لأن الله تعالى لم يخلق إلا الأجسام ثم كل جسم خلقه يخلق إما باختياره وإما بالطبع ما لا يحصى من الأعراض، فيكون بمقابلته كل فرد من أجسام العالم الذي تولى الله تعالى تخليقه ما لا يحصى كثرة مما لا يخلقه الله تعالى. والرابع: أن المجوس ما أضافوا إلى غير الصانع الحكيم إلا الشرور والقبائح تنزيها له، وهو كما أضاف الشرور والقبائح من الموت والسقم والمرض والزمانة والعمى والصم والعرج والشلل إلى غير الله تعالى أضاف أيضا المحاسن والخيرات من الفرح والسرور واللذة والحياة والصحة والبصر والسمع إلى غير الله تعالى، فضاهى المجوس في إضافة الشرور إلى غير الله تعالى، وأربى عليهم بإضافة الخيرات والمحاسن إلى غير الله. ثم العلم بحدوث الأجسام يحصل بمعرفة حدوث الأعراض، والأعراض ليست بمخلوقة الله فلم يكن الله تعالى مثبتا دلالة حدوث العالم ولا قادرًا على إثباته. وإنما أثبت ذلك غيره وهو الأجسام، وكذلك ما أثبت الرسول دلالة الصدق إذ ما...
And we only say: that it leads to the assertion of two creators, because the world is accidents and substances, and God Almighty is the Creator of substances. As for accidents, according to the saying of Mu`ammar, the head of the Mu`tazila: God Almighty has no power over anything of accidents, and He did not undertake their creation nor their existence, nor are they within His power. Rather, bodies themselves create accidents, some by their natures and some by choice. So according to him, God Almighty did not create health nor sickness, nor death nor life, nor color nor smell nor taste, nor form nor composition nor arrangement, nor motion nor stillness, nor other than that. And in this is corruption from several aspects: First: that he permitted the creation of accidents from inanimate objects and lifeless things, which have no life, power, or knowledge, while those accidents are precise, perfected acts, and permitting their creation from one who has no knowledge, power, or life nullifies the Creator being described with these attributes, because the precise, perfected act is evidence for the affirmation of these attributes, and the intellects necessarily judge it to be ignorance and foolishness where it permits precise acts from one with no life, knowledge, or power. Second: that he made partners for God Almighty in the creation of the world, because since the world was divided and only one part came from Him, and the other part came from another, the world was created by Him and by others. Then his saying is worse than the saying of the Magians in several ways: First: that the Magians only affirm one partner for the Creator, while he affirmed countless partners. Second: that he permitted the existence of precise, perfected acts from one with no life, knowledge, or power, and the Magians denied that. And he affirmed for God Almighty partners that are not living, not knowing, not powerful, while the Magians did not permit that. Third: that he made the making of every inanimate and lifeless thing greater than the making of God Almighty, because God Almighty created only bodies, then each body He creates creates — either by its choice or by nature — countless accidents, so corresponding to each individual body of the world whose creation God undertook there are countless things that God does not create. Fourth: that the Magians only attributed to other than the wise Creator evils and ugly things to exalt Him, while he — just as he attributed evils and ugly things like death, illness, disease, chronic ailment, blindness, deafness, lameness, and paralysis to other than God Almighty — also attributed good things and virtues like joy, pleasure, delight, life, health, sight, and hearing to other than God Almighty. So he matched the Magians in attributing evils to other than God, and surpassed them in attributing good things and virtues to other than God. Then knowledge of the temporal origination of bodies is obtained by knowing the temporal origination of accidents, and accidents are not created by God, so God Almighty was not the one who established the evidence for the world's temporal origination nor was He able to establish it. Rather, that was established by another, namely bodies. And similarly, the prophet did not establish the proof of truthfulness, since...
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu`tazilite Mu`ammar's position on the origin of accidents. Nasafi begins by stating that Mu`ammar's view necessarily leads to the belief in two creators. He explains that the world consists of substances (a`yan) and accidents (a`rad). According to Mu`ammar, God creates only substances, while accidents are created by the bodies themselves—some by their nature (tab`), others by choice (ikhtiyar). Thus, God has no power over accidents, and did not create any of them. Nasafi then lists several corrupt consequences of this view. The first aspect: Mu`ammar allows precise, perfected acts (af`al muhkama mutqana) to be produced by lifeless objects (mawat, jumadat) that have no life, power, or knowledge. This contradicts the evidence that such acts require a creator with life, power, and knowledge. If such acts can come from the lifeless, then the Creator's attributes of life, power, and knowledge become meaningless. The intellect clearly rejects this as ignorance and foolishness. The second aspect: Mu`ammar makes partners for God in creation. Since the world is divided into parts created by God (substances) and parts created by others (accidents), God is not the sole creator. Then Nasafi compares this to the Magians (Zoroastrians) and shows Mu`ammar's view is worse in four ways. First, the Magians only affirm one partner for the creator, while Mu`ammar affirms countless partners (since every body creates countless accidents). Second, Mu`ammar permits perfect acts from non-living, ignorant, powerless things, while the Magians deny that. Third, Mu`ammar makes the creation of lifeless things exceed God's own creation: God only creates bodies, but each body creates countless accidents, so the total of what bodies create is greater than what God creates. Fourth, the Magians only attribute evil and ugly things to another principle to exalt God, but Mu`ammar attributes both evil and good to other than God, thus exceeding the Magians in associating partners with God in goodness. Finally, Nasafi points out a theological problem: knowledge of the origin of bodies depends on knowledge of accidents. But if accidents are not created by God, then God does not establish the proof for the origin of the world; rather, bodies do. Similarly, the prophet's proof of truthfulness would not be established by God but by others. The text ends abruptly, but the argument is clear.

Key Terms
تخليق
أفعال محكمة متقنة
موات
جمادات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does Nasafi say that permitting lifeless bodies to create accidents is foolish?
A) Because lifeless bodies are too small
B) Because accidents are not real
C) Because perfect acts require a creator with knowledge and power
D) Because only God can create

Key Takeaway
Attributing perfectly ordered acts to lifeless things contradicts the evidence that such acts require a knowing, powerful creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe the precision and order in your own life—your health, your provision, the timing of events—do you truly attribute them to a Creator with knowledge and power, or do you unconsciously allow your heart to credit lifeless causes like 'luck' or 'circumstances'? Where in your daily gratitude have you omitted the Giver?
?
Nasafi says the intellect judges as ignorance and foolishness to attribute precise acts to one without life or knowledge. Where in your own thinking have you reasoned that good things simply 'happen' without acknowledging the One who alone can bring them about? Will you repent from that foolishness today?
?
The evidence of precise acts points to God's attributes. If you were to examine your own belief in God's attributes—His life, knowledge, power—are they just theoretical terms, or do you see them reflected in the order around you? What one change can you make today to align your acknowledgment of His attributes with the reality of His creation?

Second Corruption: Partners for God and Comparison with Magians
Nasafi details the second corruption and compares with Magians.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four ways in which Mu`ammar's view is worse than Magian dualism.

Core Idea
Mu`ammar's view is worse than Zoroastrian dualism in four specific ways.

After the first corruption, Nasafi presents the second: Mu`ammar makes partners for God in creation. He then compares Mu`ammar's view with that of the Magians (Zoroastrians) and shows that Mu`ammar's position is even more problematic in four distinct respects.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation for the second corruption and the four comparisons.
Look for the phrase 'شرًا من قول المجوس' (worse than the saying of the Magians).
2
Identify the four ways
Nasafi lists four points where Mu`ammar exceeds the Magians in error. Find each one.
3
Understand the emphasis
Nasafi shows that Mu`ammar's view is not just dualism but a more extreme polytheism and absurdity.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Mu`ammar's view is similar to Magian dualism, but Nasafi shows it is far worse.
Correction: Mu`ammar's view exceeds Magian dualism in number of partners, nature of partners, quantity of creation, and attribution of both good and evil.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what is the fourth way Mu`ammar's view is worse than the Magians?
A) He attributes only evils to other than God
B) He attributes both good and evil to other than God
C) He attributes only good to other than God
D) He denies the existence of good and evil

Key Takeaway
Mu`ammar's view is even more problematic than Magian dualism in four distinct ways, especially by attributing good as well as evil to other than God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself attributing a good outcome in your life to your own effort or skill, as if God was not the sole provider? How does Mu`ammar's error of crediting bodies with creation challenge your daily gratitude?
?
When you face difficulty, do you sometimes blame circumstances or people as if they have independent power to harm you? Reflect on the Magians' attribution of evil to another principle—do you ever, in your heart, assign independent power to anything besides Allah?
?
Mu`ammar's view made countless partners for God because every body creates. Consider the countless things you rely on daily: your health, your wealth, your relationships. Are you subtly treating them as independent creators of your well-being? What would it look like to see Allah as the sole creator of every accident in your life—every moment of happiness, every hardship?

Epistemological Consequence: God Does Not Establish Proof
Nasafi draws an epistemological consequence of Mu`ammar's view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Mu`ammar's view undermines the proof of the world's temporal origination and the prophet's truthfulness.

Core Idea
If accidents are not created by God, then God cannot prove the world's origin.

In this final passage, Nasafi points out a critical epistemological problem: knowledge of the world's temporal origination (huduth) depends on knowing that accidents are temporal, and that requires accidents to be created by God. But Mu`ammar denies that God creates accidents, so the proof for the world's creation is not established by God but by bodies. Similarly, the prophet's proof of truthfulness is compromised.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note that the text ends abruptly.
This passage is incomplete in the source.
2
Identify the chain of knowledge
Nasafi explains that knowledge of bodies' temporal origination comes from knowing accidents are temporal and created by God.
3
See the consequence
If accidents are not God's creation, then God did not establish that proof; bodies did. This also affects prophethood.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

والآخر أنه جعل الله تعالى شركاء في تخليق العالم إذ العالم لما كان منقسما ولم يكن منه إلا قسم واحد، وكان القسم الآخر حاصلا بغيره كان العالم مخلوقا له ولغيره، ثم كان قوله شرًا من قول المجوس بوجوه: أحدها: أن المجوس لا تثبت للصانع إلا شريكًا واحدًا، وهو أثبت ما لا يحصى من الشركاء. والثاني: أنه جوز وجود الفعل المحكم المتقن ممن لا حياة له ولا علم ولا قدرة، والمجوس أنكروا ذلك. وهو أثبت الله تعالى شركاء غير أحياء لا علماء ولا قادرين والمجوس لم يجوزوا ذلك. والثالث: أنه جعل صنع كل جماد وموات وأكثر من صنع الله تعالى، لأن الله تعالى لم يخلق إلا الأجسام ثم كل جسم خلقه يخلق إما باختياره وإما بالطبع ما لا يحصى من الأعراض، فيكون بمقابلته كل فرد من أجسام العالم الذي تولى الله تعالى تخليقه ما لا يحصى كثرة مما لا يخلقه الله تعالى. والرابع: أن المجوس ما أضافوا إلى غير الصانع الحكيم إلا الشرور والقبائح تنزيها له، وهو كما أضاف الشرور والقبائح من الموت والسقم والمرض والزمانة والعمى والصم والعرج والشلل إلى غير الله تعالى أضاف أيضا المحاسن والخيرات من الفرح والسرور واللذة والحياة والصحة والبصر والسمع إلى غير الله تعالى، فضاهى المجوس في إضافة الشرور إلى غير الله تعالى، وأربى عليهم بإضافة الخيرات والمحاسن إلى غير الله. ثم العلم بحدوث الأجسام يحصل بمعرفة حدوث الأعراض، والأعراض ليست بمخلوقة الله فلم يكن الله تعالى مثبتا دلالة حدوث العالم ولا قادرًا على إثباته. وإنما أثبت ذلك غيره وهو الأجسام، وكذلك ما أثبت الرسول دلالة الصدق إذ ما...
And we only say: that it leads to the assertion of two creators, because the world is accidents and substances, and God Almighty is the Creator of substances. As for accidents, according to the saying of Mu`ammar, the head of the Mu`tazila: God Almighty has no power over anything of accidents, and He did not undertake their creation nor their existence, nor are they within His power. Rather, bodies themselves create accidents, some by their natures and some by choice. So according to him, God Almighty did not create health nor sickness, nor death nor life, nor color nor smell nor taste, nor form nor composition nor arrangement, nor motion nor stillness, nor other than that. And in this is corruption from several aspects: First: that he permitted the creation of accidents from inanimate objects and lifeless things, which have no life, power, or knowledge, while those accidents are precise, perfected acts, and permitting their creation from one who has no knowledge, power, or life nullifies the Creator being described with these attributes, because the precise, perfected act is evidence for the affirmation of these attributes, and the intellects necessarily judge it to be ignorance and foolishness where it permits precise acts from one with no life, knowledge, or power. Second: that he made partners for God Almighty in the creation of the world, because since the world was divided and only one part came from Him, and the other part came from another, the world was created by Him and by others. Then his saying is worse than the saying of the Magians in several ways: First: that the Magians only affirm one partner for the Creator, while he affirmed countless partners. Second: that he permitted the existence of precise, perfected acts from one with no life, knowledge, or power, and the Magians denied that. And he affirmed for God Almighty partners that are not living, not knowing, not powerful, while the Magians did not permit that. Third: that he made the making of every inanimate and lifeless thing greater than the making of God Almighty, because God Almighty created only bodies, then each body He creates creates — either by its choice or by nature — countless accidents, so corresponding to each individual body of the world whose creation God undertook there are countless things that God does not create. Fourth: that the Magians only attributed to other than the wise Creator evils and ugly things to exalt Him, while he — just as he attributed evils and ugly things like death, illness, disease, chronic ailment, blindness, deafness, lameness, and paralysis to other than God Almighty — also attributed good things and virtues like joy, pleasure, delight, life, health, sight, and hearing to other than God Almighty. So he matched the Magians in attributing evils to other than God, and surpassed them in attributing good things and virtues to other than God. Then knowledge of the temporal origination of bodies is obtained by knowing the temporal origination of accidents, and accidents are not created by God, so God Almighty was not the one who established the evidence for the world's temporal origination nor was He able to establish it. Rather, that was established by another, namely bodies. And similarly, the prophet did not establish the proof of truthfulness, since...
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu`tazilite Mu`ammar's position on the origin of accidents. Nasafi begins by stating that Mu`ammar's view necessarily leads to the belief in two creators. He explains that the world consists of substances (a`yan) and accidents (a`rad). According to Mu`ammar, God creates only substances, while accidents are created by the bodies themselves—some by their nature (tab`), others by choice (ikhtiyar). Thus, God has no power over accidents, and did not create any of them. Nasafi then lists several corrupt consequences of this view. The first aspect: Mu`ammar allows precise, perfected acts (af`al muhkama mutqana) to be produced by lifeless objects (mawat, jumadat) that have no life, power, or knowledge. This contradicts the evidence that such acts require a creator with life, power, and knowledge. If such acts can come from the lifeless, then the Creator's attributes of life, power, and knowledge become meaningless. The intellect clearly rejects this as ignorance and foolishness. The second aspect: Mu`ammar makes partners for God in creation. Since the world is divided into parts created by God (substances) and parts created by others (accidents), God is not the sole creator. Then Nasafi compares this to the Magians (Zoroastrians) and shows Mu`ammar's view is worse in four ways. First, the Magians only affirm one partner for the creator, while Mu`ammar affirms countless partners (since every body creates countless accidents). Second, Mu`ammar permits perfect acts from non-living, ignorant, powerless things, while the Magians deny that. Third, Mu`ammar makes the creation of lifeless things exceed God's own creation: God only creates bodies, but each body creates countless accidents, so the total of what bodies create is greater than what God creates. Fourth, the Magians only attribute evil and ugly things to another principle to exalt God, but Mu`ammar attributes both evil and good to other than God, thus exceeding the Magians in associating partners with God in goodness. Finally, Nasafi points out a theological problem: knowledge of the origin of bodies depends on knowledge of accidents. But if accidents are not created by God, then God does not establish the proof for the origin of the world; rather, bodies do. Similarly, the prophet's proof of truthfulness would not be established by God but by others. The text ends abruptly, but the argument is clear.

Key Terms
حدوث
دلالة
صادق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what happens to the proof for the world's temporal origination if accidents are not created by God?
A) It is still valid because bodies are temporal
B) God does not establish it; bodies do
C) It becomes impossible to know
D) It is still established by God through other means

Key Takeaway
Mu`ammar's view undermines the very proof for the world's creation, shifting it from God to bodies, and jeopardizes the prophet's evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the evidence for the existence of a Creator were not ultimately from God Himself, would your certainty in His existence be shaken? Examine your own heart: do you rely more on intellectual proofs or on the direct affirmation of the Qur'an? What would it take for you to truly depend on God as the establisher of all truth?
?
The passage suggests that without God creating accidents, the proof of the world's origin shifts to bodies. In your daily life, do you sometimes attribute the signs of God's existence to secondary causes rather than to Him? How can you train yourself to see His hand in every evidence?
?
The argument extends to prophethood: the prophet's own proof is not established by him if accidents are not created by God. Does your trust in the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) rest on the inherent truth of his message or on external miracles? If the miracle were absent, would your faith remain? Reflect and commit to a deeper reliance on the message itself.

Dilemma on God's Power: Attribute of Action or Essence
Presents a dilemma: if power is attribute of action it's created; if of essence it's comprehensive, contradicting Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dilemma facing the Mu'tazila regarding God's power and why their position inevitably leads to self-contradiction.

Core Idea
Falsehood is self-refuting; truth is self-supporting.

This passage presents a powerful logical dilemma against the Mu'tazila. The author Imam al-Nasafi forces them to choose: if God's power is an attribute of action, it is created; if of essence, it must be comprehensive. Either way, their position collapses. Let us see how this argument unfolds step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own argumentative rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
The passage presents two conditional arguments. Notice each 'if' statement and what conclusion follows. The first horn: if power is originated (ḥādith) – what do they lose? The second: if it is not originated – what must they admit?
The author is showing that both options refute different Mu'tazilī claims.
3
Observe the third scenario: clinging to all
The author then considers if they try to hold both: that power is of essence and also not comprehensive. This leads to an even grosser contradiction. Notice the author's conclusion: falsehood annuls itself, truth supports itself.
This is the climax of the argument.
4
Reflect on the overarching principle
Consider the final sentence about falsehood and truth. Relate it to the dilemma: the Mu'tazila cannot consistently maintain their position because their own criteria lead to contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن زعموا أنه حادث تركوا قولهم: إنه من صفات الذات وهو قادر بنفسه وإن زعموا أنه ليس بحادث وليس من صفات الفعل بل هو من صفات الذات (وهو كان موصوفا به في الأزل) فقد زعموا أن قدرته شاملة على المقدورات أجمع وقد أبطلوا قولهم: إنه لا يقدر على أفعال خلقه ورجعوا عن هذه المسألة. وإن تمسكوا بهذه المسألة وتقروا على قولهم: إن القدرة من صفات الذات، فقد أبطلوا على أنفسهم فرقهم بين صفة الذات وصفة الفعل بما ذكروا من الفرق، وإن تمسكوا بكل ذلك فقد ناقضوا أفحش مناقضة، والباطل ينقض بعضه بعضا، والحق يؤيد بعضه بعضاً، والحمد لله على العصمة من الضلالة والتمادي في الغي والجهالة.
If they claim that it (power) is originated (ḥādith), they abandon their statement that it is an attribute of essence (ṣifa al-dhāt) and that He is powerful by Himself. And if they claim that it is not originated and not an attribute of action, but rather an attribute of essence (and He was described by it in pre-eternity), then they have claimed that His power encompasses all potentialities entirely, and they have invalidated their statement that He does not have power over the acts of His creatures, and they have recanted on this issue. And if they cling to this issue and affirm their statement that power is an attribute of essence, then they have invalidated against themselves their distinction between attribute of essence and attribute of action by the distinction they mentioned. And if they cling to all of that, then they have contradicted most grossly; falsehood annuls itself part by part, and truth supports itself part by part. Praise be to Allah for protection from misguidance and persistence in error and ignorance.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage presents a dilemma against the Mu'tazila regarding the nature of God's power (qudra). The author structures the argument as a series of conditional statements: First, if the Mu'tazila claim that power is originated (ḥādith)—because they consider it an attribute of action—then they abandon their own claim that God is powerful by His essence and that power is an attribute of essence. Second, if they claim that power is not originated and is an attribute of essence (i.e., eternal), then they must admit that God's power is comprehensive over all potentialities, including the acts of creatures, thus invalidating their position that God does not have power over creatures' acts. Third, if they persist in saying power is an attribute of essence while maintaining their original position that God lacks power over creatures' acts, they contradict their own criterion for distinguishing attributes of essence from attributes of action (since power would be affirmed for some things and negated for others, making it an attribute of action by their own definition). The author concludes that holding all these together is a gross contradiction, and falsehood is self-refuting while truth is self-supporting.

Key Terms
حادث
صفات الذات
صفات الفعل
قدرة
مقدورات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the dilemma is simply about power being created or not, without considering the Mu'tazila's own criterion for distinguishing attribute types.
Correction: The dilemma specifically targets the Mu'tazila's own distinction between attributes of essence and attributes of action. They cannot simultaneously claim power is of essence and yet limited in scope without contradicting their own definition.

Checkpoint
According to the dilemma, what happens if the Mu'tazila claim God's power is an attribute of essence?
A) They must admit that God's power is limited to His own acts.
B) They must admit that God's power encompasses all potentialities, contradicting their denial of power over creatures' acts.
C) They must say power is originated.
D) They must abandon the distinction between attributes of essence and action.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila are caught in a contradiction: if God's power is eternal, it must cover all possible acts, but they deny He has power over creatures' acts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you claim God is all-powerful, do you truly believe He has power over every choice you make today—including your sins? How does your daily behavior reflect that belief, or do you act as if some areas are outside His control? Will you consciously acknowledge His power over your next decision?
?
The Mu'tazila tried to limit God's power to protect human free will. In what area of your life do you subconsciously limit God's power because it threatens your autonomy or comfort? What would it cost you to surrender that area fully to Him, and are you willing to pay that price today?
?
Falsehood self-destructs, truth supports itself. Think of a belief you hold about God that, upon honest reflection, crumbles when examined against what this lesson teaches about His comprehensive power. Are you prepared to abandon that belief and let the truth stand on its own, even if it demands more from you?
