The Consensus Argument for One Act Under Two Powers
Nasafi uses consensus to prove one act under two powers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the consensus that Allah empowers the servant implies the permissibility of one act being under two powers.

Core Idea
Consensus proves that one act can belong to both Allah and the servant.

Imam Nasafi begins with an argument from consensus (ijmāʿ) to establish a key Maturidi principle: the same act can fall under both divine and human power. He explains that since Allah creates the servant's power, it is impossible that He would empower the servant over something outside his capacity. This leads to the logical conclusion that one and the same act can be within the power of two agents. Let's examine the precise reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the consensus premise
Read the first part of the passage where the author invokes consensus that Allah empowers the servant and creates his power. Note that this is a foundational point agreed upon by Ahl al-Sunna.
The consensus is used as a starting point, not a proof for the whole doctrine.
2
Trace the logical implication
Follow the reasoning: If Allah empowers the servant, He cannot empower him over something impossible. Since the servant's act is possible, it must be within both Allah's creative power and the servant's acquired power.
This step shows the necessity of the permissibility.
3
Identify the conclusion
The author explicitly states that the consensus leads to a proof (dalīl) for the permissibility of one maqdūr under two qādirīn. Confirm you see the Arabic phrase for this conclusion.
The conclusion is the point of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد انعقد الإجماع أن الله تعالى هو الذي يقدر العبد ويخلق قدرته، وأن القدرة ليست بداخله تحت قدرة العبد إذ لو منعت منه لما تمكن من اكتسابها عندنا وتخليقها عندهم، وإذا كان الله تعالى هو الذي يقدر العبد كان محالاً أن يقدره على ما لا قدرة له عليه على ما قررنا، فكان ذلك دليلاً على جواز دخول مقدور واحد تحت قدرة قادرين، ثم لا دليل يدل للمعتزلة على استحالة دخول مقدور واحد تحت قدرة قادرين، إلا أنهم لم يعاينوا ذلك وهم أبدا يفزعون إلى الوهم ويجعلونه عيارا للعقل والفهم وهو بعيد عن الصواب على ما قررنا. ثم قيل لهم أيش تعنون بقولكم إن دخول مقدور واحد تحت قدرة قادرين محال ؟ أتعنون دخوله من جهة الاختراع أو من جهة الاكتساب أو من جهة اكتساب و اختراع؟ فإن قالوا من جهة الاختراع فسلم تنازعهم في هذا ، وإن قالوا: من جهة الاكتساب فكذلك لأن فعلاً واحدًا لا يكون فعلا لكاسبين. وإن قالوا من جهة الاكتساب والاختراع أن يقدر على اختراعه مخترع وعلى اكتسابه مكتسب ففيه النزاع. فقيل لهم : لم قلتم ذلك وقد وقعت الخصومة فيه ثم ما أقمنا من الدلائل موجب ذلك ومن عد الممكن ممتنعا فهو جاهلي فكيف بمن عد الواجب ممتنعا؟ ثم هم إنما عدوا ذلك ممتنعا لأنهم لم يعقلوا تعلق القدرة بالمقدور إلا من جهة الاختراع وذلك لعمري محال وعند خصومهم قط لا يجوز تعلق قدرتين بجهة الاختراع بمقدور واحد وإنما الكلام وراء ذلك وهو أن قدرة واحدة لما تعلقت بمقدور بجهة الاختراع هل يجوز أن تتعلق به قدرة أخرى يكون أثرها جعل ذلك المخترع فعلا له أم لا؟ عندنا ذلك جائز وعندهم ذلك ممتنع، وقد أقمنا الدلالة على جوازه فضلاً وجوبه.
And consensus has been reached that Allah Almighty is the one who empowers the servant and creates his power, and that power is not within him under the servant's power, for if it were withheld from him he would not be able to acquire it according to us and create it according to them. And if Allah Almighty is the one who empowers the servant, it is impossible that He should empower him over what he has no power over, as we have established. So that was a proof for the permissibility of one thing within power (maqdūr) entering under the power of two powerful agents (qādirīn). Then there is no proof for the Mu'tazila of the impossibility of one thing within power entering under the power of two powerful agents, except that they have not witnessed that, and they always flee to imagination (wahm) and make it a measure for intellect and understanding, and it is far from correctness, as we have established. Then it was said to them: What do you mean by your statement that the entering of one thing within power under the power of two powerful agents is impossible? Do you mean its entering by way of creation (ikhtirāʿ) or by way of acquisition (iktisāb) or by way of acquisition and creation? If they say by way of creation, then the dispute with them in this is conceded. And if they say by way of acquisition, likewise because a single act cannot be an act of two acquirers. And if they say by way of acquisition and creation, that a creator (mukhtariʿ) has power over its creation and an acquirer (muktasib) over its acquisition, then in that lies the dispute. So it was said to them: Why did you say that, while the dispute has occurred in it, and we have established proofs that necessitate that, and whoever considers the possible impossible is an ignoramus, so how about one who considers the necessary impossible? Then they only considered that impossible because they did not conceive of the attachment of power to the thing within power except by way of creation, and that, by my life, is impossible, and according to their opponents, the attachment of two powers by way of creation to one thing within power is never permissible, and the discourse is beyond that, namely: when one power has attached to a thing within power by way of creation, is it permissible for another power to attach to it whose effect is making that created thing an act for him or not? According to us, that is permissible, and according to them, it is impossible, and we have established the proof for its permissibility, let alone its necessity.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage is a proof and definition. The author begins by invoking consensus (ijmāʿ) that Allah is the one who empowers the servant and creates his power, and that power is not within the servant's control. From this, he argues that it is permissible for one act (maqdūr) to be under the power of two agents (qādirīn). He then notes that the Mu'tazila have no proof for its impossibility except their reliance on imagination (wahm) rather than reason. Next, he clarifies the term 'impossible' by asking the Mu'tazila what they mean by 'one act under two powers being impossible.' He distinguishes two aspects: creation (ikhtirāʿ) and acquisition (iktisāb). They agree that one act cannot be created by two, and that one act cannot be acquired by two. The dispute is only over the combination: one act created by Allah and acquired by the servant. The author then criticizes the Mu'tazila for considering this impossible only because they conceive of power's attachment solely as creation, ignoring acquisition. He states that the real question is whether, after one power attaches by creation, another power can attach to make that created thing an act for the second agent. The author asserts that this is permissible, and they have demonstrated it, even its necessity.

Key Terms
قدرة
مقدور
إجماع
محال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the basis for the author's argument that one act can be under two powers?
A) Reason
B) Consensus
C) Revelation
D) Imagination

Key Takeaway
Consensus that Allah empowers the servant proves it is permissible for one act to be under two powers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly live as though every action you take is both from Allah's creation and your own acquisition? How does this belief affect the way you repent and strive to improve?
?
When you succeed, do you thank Allah while also acknowledging your effort? When you fail, do you blame yourself or feel that Allah's decree excuses you? How does this lesson challenge you to hold both together without falling into despair or arrogance?
?
If you really believed that Allah empowers you for every move you make, would you ever fear losing His support through your own sin? What would change in your daily reliance on Allah if you took this consensus argument to heart?

Refuting the Mu'tazila: Imagination vs Reason
Nasafi criticizes Mu'tazila's use of imagination and clarifies the dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's flawed reliance on imagination (wahm) and the proper clarification of what 'impossible' means in this context.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila base their denial on imagination, not reason.

After establishing the consensus-based proof, Imam Nasafi turns to refute the Mu'tazila position. He points out that they have no rational proof for their claim, only an appeal to their imagination (wahm). He then forces them to clarify what exactly they mean by 'impossible' by distinguishing between creation (ikhtirāʿ) and acquisition (iktisāb). This clarification is crucial for understanding the real point of disagreement.

Guided Path
1
Read the criticism of Mu'tazila
Read the part where the author says the Mu'tazila have no proof except imagination. Notice the phrase 'يفزعون إلى الوهم'.
This is a methodological critique.
2
Examine the three possibilities
The author asks: what do you mean by 'impossible'? He lists three cases: creation by two, acquisition by two, or creation by one and acquisition by another. Identify each.
The first two are conceded as impossible by both sides.
3
Locate the real dispute
The author states that the disagreement is only over the third case: one act created by Allah and acquired by the servant. Confirm this in the text.
This is the heart of the matter.

Source Text

And consensus has been reached that Allah Almighty is the one who empowers the servant and creates his power, and that power is not within him under the servant's power, for if it were withheld from him he would not be able to acquire it according to us and create it according to them. ?

Do you live as though your actions are solely your own, or do you constantly acknowledge that every breath and every deed is under Allah's power? The Real Dispute: Creation by Allah, Acquisition by Servant
Nasafi explains the real issue and affirms the permissibility of dual attachment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise point of controversy: whether after an act is created by Allah, another power can attach to it as an acquired act of the servant.

Core Idea
The dispute is about a single act being created by Allah and acquired by the servant.

Having narrowed down the disagreement, Imam Nasafi now argues that the Mu'tazila have no grounds to deny the possibility that one act can be created by Allah and simultaneously acquired by the servant. He criticizes them for conflating impossibility in one aspect with impossibility in another, and he asserts that the Ahl al-Sunna have provided proofs for its permissibility, even necessity.

Guided Path
1
Read the author's rebuttal
Read the passage starting 'فقيل لهم لم قلتم ذلك'. Note the rhetorical question and the accusation of considering the possible impossible.
The author is sharp in his critique.
2
Understand the Mu'tazila's error
The author says the Mu'tazila only conceive of power's attachment as creation (ikhtirāʿ), so they think it's impossible for two powers to attach in any way. Recognize this as a conceptual limitation.
They ignore the possibility of acquisition.
3
Grasp the author's positive claim
The author states the real question: after one power attaches by creation, can another power attach to make the act an acquisition? He affirms that this is permissible and even necessary, and that proofs have been given.
This is the core of the Maturidi doctrine of kasb.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد انعقد الإجماع أن الله تعالى هو الذي يقدر العبد ويخلق قدرته، وأن القدرة ليست بداخله تحت قدرة العبد إذ لو منعت منه لما تمكن من اكتسابها عندنا وتخليقها عندهم، وإذا كان الله تعالى هو الذي يقدر العبد كان محالاً أن يقدره على ما لا قدرة له عليه على ما قررنا، فكان ذلك دليلاً على جواز دخول مقدور واحد تحت قدرة قادرين، ثم لا دليل يدل للمعتزلة على استحالة دخول مقدور واحد تحت قدرة قادرين، إلا أنهم لم يعاينوا ذلك وهم أبدا يفزعون إلى الوهم ويجعلونه عيارا للعقل والفهم وهو بعيد عن الصواب على ما قررنا. ثم قيل لهم أيش تعنون بقولكم إن دخول مقدور واحد تحت قدرة قادرين محال ؟ أتعنون دخوله من جهة الاختراع أو من جهة الاكتساب أو من جهة اكتساب و اختراع؟ فإن قالوا من جهة الاختراع فسلم تنازعهم في هذا ، وإن قالوا: من جهة الاكتساب فكذلك لأن فعلاً واحدًا لا يكون فعلا لكاسبين. وإن قالوا من جهة الاكتساب والاختراع أن يقدر على اختراعه مخترع وعلى اكتسابه مكتسب ففيه النزاع. فقيل لهم : لم قلتم ذلك وقد وقعت الخصومة فيه ثم ما أقمنا من الدلائل موجب ذلك ومن عد الممكن ممتنعا فهو جاهلي فكيف بمن عد الواجب ممتنعا؟ ثم هم إنما عدوا ذلك ممتنعا لأنهم لم يعقلوا تعلق القدرة بالمقدور إلا من جهة الاختراع وذلك لعمري محال وعند خصومهم قط لا يجوز تعلق قدرتين بجهة الاختراع بمقدور واحد وإنما الكلام وراء ذلك وهو أن قدرة واحدة لما تعلقت بمقدور بجهة الاختراع هل يجوز أن تتعلق به قدرة أخرى يكون أثرها جعل ذلك المخترع فعلا له أم لا؟ عندنا ذلك جائز وعندهم ذلك ممتنع، وقد أقمنا الدلالة على جوازه فضلاً وجوبه.
And consensus has been reached that Allah Almighty is the one who empowers the servant and creates his power, and that power is not within him under the servant's power, for if it were withheld from him he would not be able to acquire it according to us and create it according to them. And if Allah Almighty is the one who empowers the servant, it is impossible that He should empower him over what he has no power over, as we have established. So that was a proof for the permissibility of one thing within power (maqdūr) entering under the power of two powerful agents (qādirīn). Then there is no proof for the Mu'tazila of the impossibility of one thing within power entering under the power of two powerful agents, except that they have not witnessed that, and they always flee to imagination (wahm) and make it a measure for intellect and understanding, and it is far from correctness, as we have established. Then it was said to them: What do you mean by your statement that the entering of one thing within power under the power of two powerful agents is impossible? Do you mean its entering by way of creation (ikhtirāʿ) or by way of acquisition (iktisāb) or by way of acquisition and creation? If they say by way of creation, then the dispute with them in this is conceded. And if they say by way of acquisition, likewise because a single act cannot be an act of two acquirers. And if they say by way of acquisition and creation, that a creator (mukhtariʿ) has power over its creation and an acquirer (muktasib) over its acquisition, then in that lies the dispute. So it was said to them: Why did you say that, while the dispute has occurred in it, and we have established proofs that necessitate that, and whoever considers the possible impossible is an ignoramus, so how about one who considers the necessary impossible? Then they only considered that impossible because they did not conceive of the attachment of power to the thing within power except by way of creation, and that, by my life, is impossible, and according to their opponents, the attachment of two powers by way of creation to one thing within power is never permissible, and the discourse is beyond that, namely: when one power has attached to a thing within power by way of creation, is it permissible for another power to attach to it whose effect is making that created thing an act for him or not? According to us, that is permissible, and according to them, it is impossible, and we have established the proof for its permissibility, let alone its necessity.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage is a proof and definition. The author begins by invoking consensus (ijmāʿ) that Allah is the one who empowers the servant and creates his power, and that power is not within the servant's control. From this, he argues that it is permissible for one act (maqdūr) to be under the power of two agents (qādirīn). He then notes that the Mu'tazila have no proof for its impossibility except their reliance on imagination (wahm) rather than reason. Next, he clarifies the term 'impossible' by asking the Mu'tazila what they mean by 'one act under two powers being impossible.' He distinguishes two aspects: creation (ikhtirāʿ) and acquisition (iktisāb). They agree that one act cannot be created by two, and that one act cannot be acquired by two. The dispute is only over the combination: one act created by Allah and acquired by the servant. The author then criticizes the Mu'tazila for considering this impossible only because they conceive of power's attachment solely as creation, ignoring acquisition. He states that the real question is whether, after one power attaches by creation, another power can attach to make that created thing an act for the second agent. The author asserts that this is permissible, and they have demonstrated it, even its necessity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if one act cannot be created by two, then it cannot be under two powers at all.
Correction: The author explains that creation and acquisition are different aspects; one act can be created by Allah and acquired by the servant.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why do the Mu'tazila consider one act under two powers impossible?
A) They have a clear proof from reason.
B) They only think of power as creation.
C) They deny the consensus.
D) They accept acquisition but reject creation.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila error is restricting power to creation; the Maturidi position is that an act can be both created by Allah and acquired by the servant.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you ever excuse yourself by thinking, 'Allah created this act, so I bear no responsibility'? How does this lesson's teaching that you acquire the act challenge your conscience? Will you repent truly, acknowledging both Allah's creation and your acquisition?
?
The Mu'tazila clung to their limited understanding of power. Is there a belief you hold about Allah or yourself that you are unwilling to question, simply because you cannot conceive of an alternative? Are you ready to submit to revelation even when your intellect struggles?
?
Your every action is created by Allah and acquired by you. Does that thought terrify you, or does it make you more mindful of the weight of your choices? Starting now, will you pause before each action and remember that both Allah's power and your responsibility are real?

Refuting the Impossibility of Two Wills for One Action
Author refutes opponent's claim that two wills cannot coexist for one motion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author argues that two wills can coexist for a single action, and why the opponent's analogy fails due to confusion between necessary and inferential knowledge.

Core Idea
Two wills can coexist for one act; their loci differ, and the opponent confuses spatial occupancy with causal agency.

This passage presents a nuanced counter-argument from the Maturidi tradition against an opponent who claims that two wills cannot simultaneously direct a single body. The author unpacks the opponent's reasoning, introduces key distinctions between necessary and inferential knowledge, and defends the possibility of multiple agents willing the same effect. Read carefully to see how the argument unfolds.

Guided Path
1
Understand the opponent's concession
The opponent concedes that God can create two equal men but claims they cannot both will to move a stone. Note the author's strategy: starting from accepted premises.
This sets the stage for the author's counter-argument.
2
Trace the main objection
The author questions why if one man wills to move the stone, the other cannot. He points out no opposition exists because wills have different loci and no corruption of power.
The key point is that the wills are in separate subjects.
3
Analyze the analogy with the intermediate part
The opponent's analogy involves two bodies moving to the same point, impossible due to spatial occupation. The author distinguishes this from two wills acting on one object where no spatial conflict exists.
Movement to a point involves physical collision; willing does not.
4
Distinguish necessary vs. inferential knowledge
The author argues the impossibility of two occupying one space is necessary, while the impossibility of one act having two agents is not necessary but inferential, leading to absurdity if accepted.
Thus the analogy fails because it mixes different types of knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول له: إنك سلمت أن تخليق الله رجلين مستويين في القوة والبنية من جملة الممكنات، وإنما جعلت المستحيل وجود إرادتهما لتحريك ذلك الجسم ويجوز وجود إرادة كل واحد منهما لتحريك الجسم والاعتماد عليه للتحريك على الانفراد فبعد ذلك نقول: إذا وجدت من أحدهما إرادة تحريك الحجر لماذا امتنع وجود إرادة تحريك ذلك الآخر، ولا مضادة بين الإرادتين والفعلين لتفرق محليهما، ولا فساد لمحل قدرة أحدهما عند ورود إرادة الآخر، فإن تعلق بالمثل الذي ضربه قلنا هناك: إنما استحال وجود إرادة الاثنين لأن إرادتهما للتحرك إلى الجزء المتوسط لا تقع إلا مع حكمها وهو التحرك، ولما استحال شغلهما ذلك الجزء المتوسط استحال وجود ما لا يقع إلا مع حركتيهما وهو الإرادة ولا استحالة في وجود الاعتماد من الاثنين على ذلك الجسم وتحريكهما إياه، فإن نوزعنا في كون إرادة التحرك التحرك مع نثبت ذلك بالدليل على أنا بنينا ذلك على أصلنا لدفع الإلزام للإلزام على الخصم وهو مستقيم، ولأن العلم باستحالة شغل اثنين جزءًا واحدًا من الممكن ضروري والعلم باستحالة دخول مقدور واحد تحت قدرة قادرين ليس بضروري لو كان لكان استدلاليًا وبإفضائه إلى جعل ما هو ممكن ممتنعا علم بطلان الاستدلال، وفيما استشهد به الأمر بخلافه، لأن ما علم ضرورة لا يتصور خطأه وبطلانه، فلا يمكن جعل أحدهما نظير للآخر، والله الموفق.
Then we say to him: You have conceded that God's creating two men equal in strength and constitution is among possible things, and you only deemed impossible the existence of their will to move that body, while permitting the existence of each one's will to move the body and relying on it for moving individually. Then after that we say: When will to move the stone exists from one of them, why is the existence of will to move from the other prevented? There is no opposition between the two wills and the two actions because their loci are different, nor is there corruption of the locus of one's power upon the occurrence of the other's will. If he adheres to the analogy he gave, we say there: The existence of the will of both is impossible only because their will to move toward the intermediate part does not occur except with its effect, which is movement. And since their occupying that intermediate part is impossible, the existence of what does not occur except with their two movements—namely the will—is impossible. But there is no impossibility in the existence of reliance from both on that body and their moving it. If it is disputed whether will to move is movement, we establish that by evidence, because we built that on our principle to counter the opponent's objection, and it is sound. And because knowledge of the impossibility of two occupying a single part of what is possible is necessary, and knowledge of the impossibility of a single potential object falling under the power of two powerful beings is not necessary; if it were, it would be inferential. And its leading to making what is possible impossible shows the invalidity of the inference. And in what he cited, the matter is contrary, because what is known necessarily cannot be conceived as erroneous or invalid, so one cannot be made analogous to the other. And God is the giver of success.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an opponent's argument that two wills cannot simultaneously exist for moving one body. The author begins by restating the opponent's conceded premise: God can create two identical men, but the opponent claims their wills cannot coexist to move a single body. The author then argues that if one man wills to move a stone, there is no reason why the other cannot also will to move it, since there is no opposition between the wills (different loci) and no corruption of one's power locus by the other's will. The opponent's analogy involving movement to an intermediate part is rejected because that case involves necessary coincidence of will and movement, and the impossibility of two bodies occupying the same space. But here, the two wills have distinct loci, and the object itself can receive actions from both. The author further distinguishes necessary knowledge (two bodies cannot occupy one space) from inferential knowledge (one act cannot have two agents), and argues the opponent's inference leads to absurdity. The passage concludes by asserting that the opponent's attempted analogy fails because necessary knowledge cannot be refuted.

Key Terms
إرادة
قدرة
مقدور
محل
تحريك
جزء متوسط
ضروري
استدلالي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Two wills cannot simultaneously direct a single action because they would conflict.
Correction: Two wills can coexist if they have distinct loci (subjects) and there is no spatial or causal impediment; the alleged conflict confuses spatial occupancy with causal agency.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why does the opponent's analogy of two men moving toward an intermediate part fail to prove the impossibility of two wills for moving a stone?
A) Because in the analogy the will and movement are inseparable, and the intermediate part cannot be occupied by two, unlike the stone which can be moved by two wills.
B) Because the opponent's analogy involves necessary motion while the stone case is voluntary.
C) Because the stone is inanimate and cannot be moved by two wills.
D) Because the two men are not equal in strength.

Key Takeaway
The opponent's analogy fails because it conflates the necessary impossibility of spatial co-occupancy with the non-necessary, inferential claim that a single act cannot have multiple agents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a theological or logical objection that seems impossible at first, do you immediately reject it like the opponent in this passage, or do you pause to examine whether a false analogy is at play? Think of a specific belief you hold that might be based on confusing necessary and inferential knowledge. Will you commit to re-examining it this week?
?
The author argues that two wills can coexist without conflict when their loci differ. How does this reality affect your view of God's sovereignty and human free will? Do you sometimes feel your will is in competition with God's? How would your daily decisions change if you truly believed both can act together without opposition?
?
Is there an area of your religious practice where you treat a non-necessary interpretation as if it were absolutely necessary, causing you to condemn others who differ? What step will you take to distinguish between what is clearly known (ضروري) and what is based on inference (استدلالي) in that matter?

Refuting the Objection: One Act Cannot Be from Two Agents
Nasafi refutes the objection using the creation-acquisition distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi refutes the objection that one act cannot be from two agents by distinguishing between God's creating act and the servant's acquired act.

Core Idea
The same act is not from two agents in the same respect.

In this passage, Nasafi tackles a key objection raised against the Maturidi view on human agency. He argues that the objection misapplies the concept of 'act' by failing to distinguish between God's eternal act of creation and the servant's acquired act. He supports his position by citing the views of Abu Isa al-Barghouthi and Abu Hasan al-Ash'ari, and by examining examples like motion and blackness.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and Nasafi's initial response
Read the first part where the opponent's claim is stated and Nasafi says it doesn't apply.
Notice how he immediately clarifies that Maturidis do not say one act is for two agents.
2
Understand the distinction between act as creation and act as acquisition
Focus on Nasafi's explanation that God's act is an eternal attribute and the servant's act is His creation in terms of type, while the servant's acquisition is considered under the generic name.
This distinction is key to the refutation.
3
Examine the examples of motion, blackness, and speech
See how Nasafi uses these examples to show that the same attribute cannot subsist in two loci, so two agents cannot share one act.
The description 'agent' does not require the act to subsist in the agent.
4
Recognize the rhetorical conclusion
Nasafi concludes that the objection is obfuscation meant for the weak.
Appreciate the careful logical exposition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعالى من اتباع الوساوس والوقوع في ترهات البسابس (۱) ، ومما (٢) يصولون على (۳) خصومهم بقولهم : لوجاز أن يكون فعل واحد فعلاً لفاعلين جاز أن يكون (٤) قول واحد قولاً لقائلين . يبطل بهذا ( ) أيضًا على ما قررنا أن الحركة الواحدة عندهم فعل لفاعلين ولم ينفعهم التجاهل ثم نقول : إن هذا الكلام لا يتوجه علينا فإنا لا نقول إن فعلاً واحدًا يكون فعلاً لفاعلين البتة، فإن ما هو فعل الله تعالى هو صفة أزلية قائمة بذاته وليس بفعل للعبد وما هو فعل للعبد ليس بفعل الله تعالى بل هو مفعوله، ثم الفعل اسم عام وله أسماء وأنواع ، فإن كان ما خلقه الله تعالى من فعل العبد ضربًا فهو معقول الله تعالى وفعل العبد باعتبار اسم جنسه و ضربه باعتبار اسم نوعه، وكذا لو خلق قول العبد فهو مفعول الله تعالى وهو فعل العبد باعتبار اسم جنسه وقوله باعتبار اسم نوعه، وكذا على قول أبي عيسى البرغوث وأبي حسن الأشعري لا يتوجه الإلزام فإنهما لا يجعلان فعلاً واحد لفاعلين بل لا فعل إلا لله تعالى، فأما العبد فله الكسب وقول العبد اسم لكسبه فيكون قوله فعلاً الله تعالى كسبًا للعبد، ثم ما هو من الأسماء للكسب يكون راجعا إلى من قام به الكسب فإن بكونه فاعلاً ليس من شرطه قيام الفعل بالفاعل عندهم، ويحتجون عليكم في ذلك بالحركة والصفات الضرورية، فإن الفاعل للحركة الضرورية خالقها والمتحرك بها محلها، ولا يتصور اتصاف ذاتين بأنهما متحركان بحركة واحدة لاستحالة قياسها بذاتين، وكذا هذا في السواد فإن فاعله هو الله تعالى والأسود به المحل (۱) القابل له، وكذا في غيره من الصفات، فعلاً وكذا لا يلزم النجار وعبد الله بن سعيد (۲) والقلانسي (۳) وغيرهم حيث يجوزون لفاعلين أحدهما خالق والآخر مكتسب لأنهم (٤) لا يشترطون قيام الخلق بالخالق، وأنتم ساعدتموهم ويشترطون قيام الفعل الذي هو كسب بالمكتسب، وأنتم أيضا ساعدتموهم ) (۹) في ذلك، والقول اسم للكسب الخاص كالحركة والسواد وغيرهما فيكون الموصوف به المحل لا غير فأما من حيث إن القول تتعلق به قدرة الخالق وقدرة المكتسب لا تفارق غيره من الأفعال غير أن الموصوف باسمه النوعي من قام به على ما قررنا. وبالوقوف على High above from following evil whispers and falling into the nonsense of shu'um (evil omens). And among what they assault their opponents with is their saying: 'If it were possible that one act be an act of two agents, then it would be possible that one statement be a statement of two speakers.' This is invalidated also according to what we established: that a single motion according to them is an act of two agents, and ignorance does not benefit them. Then we say: This speech does not apply to us, for we do not say that one act is an act of two agents at all. For what is an act of God Almighty is an eternal attribute subsisting in His essence, and it is not an act of the servant. And what is an act of the servant is not an act of God Almighty, but rather it is His effected thing. Then 'act' is a general noun, and it has names and types. So if what God Almighty created from the servant's act is a type, then it is intelligible as God's act and the servant's act in consideration of its generic name, and its type in consideration of its specific name. Similarly, if He created the servant's speech, it is an effected thing of God Almighty and it is the servant's act in consideration of its generic name, and his speech in consideration of its specific name. Similarly, according to the view of Abu Isa al-Barghouthi and Abu Hasan al-Ash'ari, the objection does not apply, for they do not make one act for two agents; rather, there is no act except for God Almighty. As for the servant, he has acquisition (kash), and the servant's speech is a name for his acquisition, so his speech is God's act and an acquisition for the servant. Then what is from the names for acquisition refers to the one in whom the acquisition subsists. So if it is striking, the striker is the one in whom it subsists, not the one who created it; and the mover is the one in whom motion subsists, not the one who created it; and the speaker is the one in whom speech subsists, not the one who created it. It is inconceivable for any of these meanings to subsist except in one locus, so two are not described by it. As for being described as 'agent' (fa'il), it is not a condition according to them that the act subsist in the agent. And they argue against you in that by motion and necessary attributes. For the agent of necessary motion is its creator, and the one moving by it is its locus. It is inconceivable for two essences to be described as moving with one motion because of the impossibility of it subsisting in two essences. Similarly in blackness: its agent is God Almighty, and the black thing is the locus receptive to it. Similarly for other attributes. And similarly, the objection does not apply to al-Najjar, Abdullah ibn Sa'id, al-Qallanisi, and others, where they allow two agents—one creator and the other an acquirer—because they do not require creation (khalq) to subsist in the creator, and you have supported them. And they require the act which is acquisition (kash) to subsist in the acquirer, and you have also supported them in that. And 'speech' is a name for the particular acquisition like motion, blackness, etc., so the one described by it is the locus, no other. As for the fact that speech is related to the power of the Creator and the power of the acquirer, it does not differ from other acts, except that the one described by its specific name is the one in whom it subsists, according to what we established. Upon understanding this exposition, it becomes clear that this is obfuscation and deception upon the weak. And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection. The author first states the opposing argument: if one act could be from two agents, then one statement could be from two speakers—a claim the opponents use against the Maturidi view. Nasafi then explains why this does not apply to his school. He clarifies that Maturidis do not say a single act comes from two agents in the same sense. God’s act is an eternal attribute subsisting in His essence, whereas the servant’s act is God’s creation but is attributed to the servant under the aspect of its generic type. He supports this by citing Abu Isa al-Barghouthi and al-Ash'ari, who also distinguish between creation and acquisition. He then argues that attributes like motion and blackness can only subsist in one locus, so two agents cannot share the same act. The description 'agent' does not require the act to subsist in the agent. He further notes that opponents like al-Najjar and others avoid the objection by not requiring creation to subsist in the creator. Nasafi concludes that the objection is a mere obfuscation aimed at the weak.

Key Terms
فعل (fi'l)
فاعل (fa'il)
خلق (khalq)
كسب (kash)
قول (qawl)
حركة (haraka)
صفة أزلية (sifa azaliyya)
محل (mahall)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidis deny any human action.
Correction: They affirm human acquisition (kash) while God creates the act.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the objection that one act cannot be from two agents not apply to the Maturidi position?
A) Because Maturidis believe in two distinct simultaneous acts.
B) Because they distinguish between the act as God’s creation and the act as the servant’s acquisition.
C) Because they deny that the servant has any act.
D) Because they accept the objection.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi refutes the objection by showing that God's creative act and the servant's acquired act are not the same act in the same sense.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever acted as though your good deeds originate entirely from you, forgetting that God is the ultimate creator of your actions? How can you reconcile your sense of agency with total dependence on God starting today?
?
The objection pairs one act with two agents. In your life, where do you try to claim sole authorship of your successes while blaming God for your failures? How will you acknowledge God's creation in every moment, even in your achievements?
?
Nasafi says the objection is 'obfuscation upon the weak.' Where are you weak in your understanding of divine agency, and what specific step will you take to strengthen your conviction that both God's creation and your acquisition are real?

Proving That Creating Evil Is Not Evil
Al-Nasafi proves that creating evil is not evil using two proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why creating what humans consider evil is not actually evil, based on the proofs that all creation is from God and God is wise in all He does.

Core Idea
What seems evil to us is divine wisdom beyond our grasp.

Al-Nasafi tackles a major Mu'tazilite objection: if God is wise, how can He create evil? He offers two intertwined proofs. The first is that only God creates, so every event is His act. The second is that His wisdom covers all, so nothing He creates can be truly foolish. This reframes the debate: the problem is not God's action but our limited understanding.

Guided Path
1
Review the two proofs referenced
Al-Nasafi refers to two earlier proofs: (1) the slave has an act but not the power of origination, and (2) the statement that causing evil is evil is false. Understand that these set the stage.
If you haven't studied the earlier lessons, grasp that only God creates ex nihilo.
2
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage slowly, then the literal translation. Notice how al-Nasafi builds his argument: from divine creation to divine wisdom.
3
Identify the two logical consequences
The proof has two prongs: (a) every contingent event is by God's origination, and (b) God is wise in everything He brings into existence. See how these together refute the Mu'tazila.
4
Understand the conclusion
Al-Nasafi concludes that the Mu'tazila are ignorant of true wisdom and foolishness. Their judgment is based on lack of insight, and they claim to know all divine wisdoms, which is absurd.
Human intellects are limited; we cannot fathom all divine purposes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا من تمسك بالدليلين أعنى ما مر أن العبد له فعل وليست له قدرة الاختراع وعرف فساد قولهم إن إيجاد القبيح قبيح وإيجاد السفه سفه لأن الدليل الذي دل أن لا اختراع إلا من الله تعالى يوجب أن كل حادث كان باختراعه، وقيام الدليل على كونه عز وجل حكيمًا يوجب أنه تعالى في جميع ما أوجده حكيم، قبيحاً كان ذلك الموجد أو حسنًا سفها كان أو حكمة، وعرف بذلك أن ما يظنه المعتزلة حكمًا وسفها ليس كما ظنوا، وأنهم جاهلون بحقيقة الحكمة والسفه إذ الجهل عليهم جائز ممكن والخطأ على ما أقمنا من الدليل ممتنع، والحاصل أنهم يحكمون بكون ما لا اطلاع لهم على وجه الحكمة بالسفه، وهذا جهل إذ عقولهم قاصرة عن كثير من الحكم البشرية، ودعوى الاطلاع على جميع الحكم الربوبية دعوى ظهر فسادها للبداهة، والله الموفق.
And likewise, whoever adheres to the two proofs – I mean that which passed, that the slave has an act but does not have the power of origination, and knows the corruption of their statement that causing evil is evil and causing foolishness is foolishness – because the proof that indicates that there is no creation except from God Almighty necessitates that every contingent event is by His origination; and the establishment of the proof that He, mighty and majestic, is wise necessitates that He is wise in all that He brings into existence, whether that brought into existence is ugly or good, foolishness or wisdom. And it is known thereby that what the Mu'tazila deem wisdom and foolishness is not as they think, and that they are ignorant of the reality of wisdom and foolishness, since ignorance of them is possible and feasible, and error, according to the proof we have established, is impossible. And the summary is that they judge that which they have no acquaintance with regarding the aspect of wisdom to be foolishness, and this is ignorance, for their intellects are deficient with respect to many of the human wisdoms, and the claim of acquaintance with all the divine wisdoms is a claim whose corruption is manifest to intuition. And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage provides a proof that creating evil is not evil. Al-Nasafi refers to two earlier proofs: (1) the slave has act but not power of origination (ikh tirā'), and (2) the Mu'tazila claim that causing evil is evil is false. The proof proceeds: The daleel that nothing exists except by God's creation implies that every contingent event (hādith) is brought into existence by God. The daleel that God is wise implies that He is wise in everything He creates, whether it appears ugly or foolish to us. Therefore, what the Mu'tazila call wisdom and foolishness is mistaken; they are ignorant of the true nature of wisdom and foolishness. Their judgment is based on lack of insight into the divine wisdom, which is beyond human intellects. The claim to know all divine wisdoms is evidently false. Thus God's creating what seems evil is not actually evil.

Key Terms
إيجاد القبيح
الحكمة
السفه
الاختراع
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if an act appears evil, God cannot do it, because God is wise and would not create evil.
Correction: Al-Nasafi argues that wisdom is not limited to human perception; God's wisdom encompasses all His creation, so what seems evil to us may have a wise purpose unknown to us.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi's proof, why is creating what appears evil not actually evil?
A) Because evil is only a human concept and God is beyond good and evil.
B) Because all contingent events are from God's creation and God is wise in all He creates.
C) Because the Mu'tazila are wrong about everything.
D) Because God's power overrides His wisdom.

Key Takeaway
God's creation of what seems evil is not evil because He is wise in all He does, and our limited intellects cannot comprehend all divine wisdoms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When did you last blame God for a tragedy or difficulty, thinking it was foolish or evil? How does this lesson challenge you to reconsider that judgment?
?
Your intellect cannot fully grasp even human wisdoms, yet you often assume you know what is best. What would it look like to truly trust God's wisdom in an area where you are currently struggling to submit?
?
The Mu'tazila claimed to know divine wisdom comprehensively. In what areas of your life do you act as if you have complete knowledge of God's plan, and what step can you take today to embrace humility before His wisdom?

Proof of God's Wisdom and Refutation of Mu'tazila
Proof that God is wise in all actions and refutation of Mu'tazila's misunderstanding.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God is wise in all His actions and why the Mu'tazila's claim to judge divine wisdom is mistaken.

Core Idea
God is wise in all actions; human reason cannot fully comprehend divine wisdom.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi establishes a fundamental principle of Maturidi theology: God's wisdom is all-encompassing and does not depend on human judgments of benefit or harm. He then refutes the Mu'tazila who presumed to label some divine actions as 'folly' based on their limited understanding. This lesson will unpack both the positive proof and the critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and its translation
Let the text speak first before breaking it down. Pay attention to the key terms.
Pay attention to the term 'حكيم' and 'سفه'.
2
Identify the core claim about God's wisdom
Find where the author states that God is wise in all His creations, regardless of how they appear.
The phrase 'في جميع ما أوجده حكيم' is key.
3
Understand the refutation of the Mu'tazila
Notice how the author argues that the Mu'tazila are ignorant of the true nature of wisdom and folly, and that their judgment is based on ignorance.
The passage says their intellects are incapable of even many human wisdoms.
4
Reflect on the limits of human reason
Consider why human reason cannot fully judge divine actions, and what 'بداهة' (intuition) tells us about the claim to know all divine wisdoms.
This connects to the broader Maturidi view on the limits of reason.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدليل على كونه عز وجل حكيمًا يوجب أنه تعالى في جميع ما أوجده حكيم، قبيحاً كان ذلك الموجد أو حسنًا سفها كان أو حكمة، وعرف بذلك أن ما يظنه المعتزلة حكمًا وسفها ليس كما ظنوا، وأنهم جاهلون بحقيقة الحكمة والسفه إذ الجهل عليهم جائز ممكن والخطأ على ما أقمنا من الدليل ممتنع، والحاصل أنهم يحكمون بكون ما لا اطلاع لهم على وجه الحكمة بالسفه، وهذا جهل إذ عقولهم قاصرة عن كثير من الحكم البشرية، ودعوى الاطلاع على جميع الحكم الربوبية دعوى ظهر فسادها للبداهة، والله الموفق.
The proof of His being, may He be mighty and majestic, wise requires that He, exalted, is wise in all that He has brought into existence, whether that which is brought into existence is evil or good, folly or wisdom. And it is known by that that what the Mu'tazila suppose to be wisdom and folly is not as they supposed, and that they are ignorant of the reality of wisdom and folly, since ignorance is possible and permissible for them, and error is impossible according to the proof we have established. The upshot is that they judge as folly what they have no insight into its aspect of wisdom, and this is ignorance, because their intellects are incapable of many human wisdoms, and the claim of insight into all divine wisdoms is a claim whose corruption is evident to intuition. And God is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage is a proof of God's wisdom and a refutation of the Mu'tazila's misunderstanding of wisdom and folly. The author begins by stating that the proof that God is wise entails that He is wise in all His creations, whether the created thing is considered evil or good, or whether it appears as folly or wisdom. Because God is wise, everything He does is wise by definition. From this it becomes clear that what the Mu'tazila think of as wisdom and folly is not as they think—they are ignorant of the true nature of wisdom and folly, since ignorance is possible for them, while error is impossible for God given the proof already established. The author then explains that the Mu'tazila judge as foolish that whose aspect of wisdom they do not perceive, which is itself an act of ignorance, because human intellects are incapable of grasping even many human wisdoms, let alone divine wisdoms. Claiming to have insight into all divine wisdoms is a claim whose falsity is obvious to common sense (badāha). The passage ends with a prayer for divine assistance.

Key Terms
حكيم
حكمة
سفه
معتزلة
بداهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila believe that they can identify which divine actions are foolish based on their own reason.
Correction: This passage argues that human intellects are incapable of grasping all divine wisdoms, so judging God's actions as foolish is ignorance.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the consequence of the Mu'tazila's claim to know all divine wisdoms?
A) It is a claim whose corruption is obvious to intuition.
B) It is a valid claim supported by scripture.
C) It is a claim that only the prophets can make.
D) It is a claim that the Mu'tazila did not actually make.

Key Takeaway
God is wise in all His actions, and human intellect cannot fully comprehend divine wisdom, so we must not presume to label any divine act as folly.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that something in your life seemed pointless or unfair, doubting God's wisdom in it? How does this passage challenge you to reconsider your judgment and trust His wisdom instead?
?
This lesson says that human intellect is 'incapable of many human wisdoms' – let alone divine. In what specific area of your life are you still trusting your own reasoning over God's decree? What would it look like to surrender that area to His wisdom today?
?
The Mu'tazila claimed to know all divine wisdoms. Where in your own heart do you find a similar arrogance – assuming you understand why God allows certain trials or commands? Confess that to yourself now and ask Allah to grant you humility and submission.

The Ahl al-Hadith Definition of Evil and Folly
Definition of evil and folly with an illustrative example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that according to Ahl al-Hadith, evil and folly are defined by prohibition, and since God is not subject to any prohibition, His actions cannot be evil or foolish.

Core Idea
Evil is defined by prohibition; God is above all prohibitions.

The passage begins by establishing the core definition of evil and folly according to the majority of Ahl al-Hadith theologians. It then provides a striking example to illustrate that divine actions cannot be judged by human moral categories.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the definition and the example.
Pay attention to the phrase 'ما ورد عنه النهي ولا نهي'—it may appear ambiguous but sets the foundation.
2
Identify the definition of evil and folly
Notice that evil/folly is defined as that which is prohibited. God has no prohibition, so His acts are neither evil nor foolish.
3
Examine the example of the adulterous slave
The example shows God allowing a slave to commit adultery despite having the power to prevent it. In human context this would be evil, but not for God because He is not under command.
This example is key for later refutations.
4
Understand the implication
Realize that moral categories apply only to those under divine command, not to the Lawgiver Himself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول متبرعين في ذلك على الخصوم وفاتحين طريق العلم على المسترشدين، وهو أن القبيح والسفه عند الجمهور من متكلمي أهل الحديث هو ما ورد عنه النهي ولا نهي ولا حد على الله تعالى فلا يكون فيما يفعل مرتكبًا نهيًا ولا مجاوزا حدًا حد له ولا رسماً رسم له، فلم يكن فيما يفعل سفيها ولا فعله قبيحًا، ولهذا لم يكن تركه عبده يزني بأمته مع القدرة على المنع وتخليقه في الآلة الشدة والقوة علمه أنه مع يزني بأمته قبيحًا ولا سفها، وإن كان ذلك في الشاهد قبيحًا لكون الشاهد تحت أمر خالقه ونهيه واستحالة ذلك على الله تعالى. وبهذا يفرقون بين الشاهد والغائب، ويأبون تسوية المعتزلة بين الشاهد والغائب وتعليقهم وجه الحكمة على النفع إذ من مذهب المعتزلة أن ما قبح في الشاهد يقبح في الغائب من غير نظر إلى المعنى ويقولون: لابد لكون الفعل حكمة من نفع يوجد فيه إما للفاعل وإما لغيره، وما خلا عن النفع فهو سفه، ويتعلقون أن في الشاهد هكذا. وإنما تلقنوا هذا من إخوانهم الثنوية فبنوا المذهب على قواعدهم ونسجوا على منوالهم، فزعموا أن إيجاد القبيح قبيح وأن ما خلا عن النفع فهو سفه وتعلقوا بالشاهد، غير أن الثنوية يزعمون أن ما لا منفعة فيه للفاعل فهو قبيح، ولهذا زعموا أن كل فعل للنور فهو في نفع نفسه هو وخلاصه عن وثائق الظلمة، فصوبت المعتزلة الثنوية في هاتين القاعدتين وزعمت أن إيجاد القبيح قبيح وهو سفه، غير أنهم خالفوا الثنوية في تخليق الأجسام المستخبثة المستقذرة والأعيان الضارة، فزعمت الثنوية أنها لما كانت قبيحة كان إيجادها قبيحا وهي محدثة فلابد لها من صانع آخر، وأنكرت المعتزلة قبحها مع شهادة الله تعالى بخبثها وتشبيه الكفر الخبيث بها بقوله: ﴿ وَمَثَلُ كَلِمَةٍ خَبِيثَةٍ ﴾ وزعمت أن القردة والخنازير وإبليس - عليهم اللعنة - ومردة الشياطين كلهم أشياء مستحسنة لا خبث فيها ولا قبح، وإنما القبح في الأفعال، فأنكروا أن يكون الله تعالى خالقًا لها، ولهذا أنكروا حرمة الأعيان النازلة من حرمة الأفعال منزلة الحفظ من الحماية لما لا حرمة إلا بوصف القبح ولا قبح في الأعيان لما أنها لو كانت قبيحة لم يكن إيجادها حكمة، وكذا خالفوا الثنوية في تعليق الحكمة بنفع الفاعل نفسه بل علقوها بمطلق النفع إما للفاعل وإما لغيره، وتمسك الفريقان - أعني المعتزلة والثنوية - في الأمرين جميعًا أعني - أن إيجاد القبيح قبيح وأن الفعل الخالي عن النفع سفه – بالشاهد، فنوقضت المعتزلة بما بينا أن الباري يرى عبده يزني بأمته ويخلى بينهما مع القدرة
Then we say, volunteering in that against the opponents and opening the way of knowledge to the seekers of guidance, that evil and folly according to the majority of the theologians of the People of Hadith is that concerning which there is prohibition and no prohibition, and no limit upon God, so He is not in what He does committing a prohibition nor exceeding a limit set for Him nor a boundary drawn for Him. Thus He is not foolish in what He does, nor is His act evil. Because of this, it is not considered evil or folly that He leaves His slave to commit adultery with his slave-girl, despite His ability to prevent him and His creation in him of the instrument of severity and strength, though He knows that he will commit adultery with his slave-girl, even if that would be evil in the witnessed world because the witnessed is under the command and prohibition of its Creator, and that is impossible for God. And by this they distinguish between the witnessed and the unseen, and they reject the Mu'tazila's equation of the witnessed and the unseen and their attachment of the aspect of wisdom to benefit, since it is the doctrine of the Mu'tazila that what is evil in the witnessed is evil in the unseen without regard to the meaning, and they say: For an act to be wisdom, there must be a benefit found in it either for the agent or for another, and whatever is devoid of benefit is folly, and they cling to that being the case in the witnessed. And they only learned this from their brothers the Dualists, so they built the doctrine on their principles and wove on their loom. They claimed that bringing evil into existence is evil and that what is devoid of benefit is folly, and they clung to the witnessed. However, the Dualists claim that what has no benefit for the agent is evil, and for this reason they claim that every act of light is for its own benefit and its deliverance from the bonds of darkness. So the Mu'tazila approved the Dualists in these two principles and claimed that bringing evil into existence is evil and it is folly, except that they disagreed with the Dualists concerning the creation of foul and filthy bodies and harmful substances. The Dualists claimed that since they are evil, their creation is evil, and they are originated, so they must have another creator. The Mu'tazila denied their evil despite God's testimony to their foulness and His likening of foul unbelief to them in His saying: 'And the example of a foul word...' and they claimed that apes, pigs, Iblis (may curse be upon them), and the rebellious devils are all commendable things with no foulness or evil in them, and that evil is only in actions. So they denied that God created them, and for this reason they denied the prohibition of the essences that descend from the prohibition of actions in the manner of protection from immunity, since there is no prohibition except by the attribute of evil, and there is no evil in essences because if they were evil, their creation would not be wisdom. Likewise, they disagreed with the Dualists in attaching wisdom to the benefit of the agent himself, but rather they attached it to absolute benefit, either for the agent or for another. And both groups—I mean the Mu'tazila and the Dualists—clung in both matters, i.e., that bringing evil into existence is evil and that an act devoid of benefit is folly, to the witnessed. So the Mu'tazila were refuted by what we have shown: that the Creator sees His slave commit adultery with his slave-girl and leaves them both while having the power
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage is a combined definition, refutation, and historical analysis. It begins by stating the definition of evil (qabīḥ) and folly (safah) according to the majority of the theologians of Ahl al-Hadith: evil and folly are what is prohibited (or what is subject to a prohibition? The text has 'النهي ولا نهي' which is unclear but likely means 'prohibition and no prohibition'—perhaps a corruption). The key point is that God is not subject to any command or prohibition, so He cannot commit evil or folly. The author gives an example: if God allows His slave to commit adultery with his slave-girl despite having the power to prevent it, that is not evil in God's case, even though it would be evil in the human context because humans are under divine command. This establishes the distinction between the witnessed (shāhid) and the unseen (ghā'ib): the Mu'tazila wrongly equate them and attach wisdom to benefit. The author then traces the Mu'tazila's error to the Dualists (Thunawiyya), who claimed that bringing evil into existence is evil and that an act devoid of benefit is folly. The Dualists held that any act without benefit to the agent is evil, leading them to posit a second creator for evil things. The Mu'tazila accepted these two principles but disagreed with the Dualists about the foulness of certain substances: they denied that God creates foul things like apes, pigs, and Iblis, claiming these are actually commendable and that evil is only in actions. They also disagreed with the Dualists' restriction of benefit to the agent, extending it to benefit for others. Both groups, however, rely on analogy from the witnessed world. The passage ends with a refutation: the Mu'tazila are refuted by the example of God leaving a slave to commit adultery—He could prevent it but does not, yet it is not evil because He is not subject to prohibition.

Key Terms
قبيح
سفه
شاهد
غائب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People think God's actions can be deemed evil based on human moral standards.
Correction: God is not under any command or prohibition, so His actions are beyond such judgments.

Checkpoint
According to the Ahl al-Hadith definition presented in this passage, what makes an act evil or foolish?
A) Its lack of benefit.
B) Its being prohibited by God for His creatures.
C) Its intrinsic foulness.
D) Its being done with ignorance.

Key Takeaway
Evil is defined by prohibition; God, as the Lawgiver, is not subject to prohibition, so His actions cannot be evil.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see evil in the world, do you secretly question God's justice? How does this lesson's definition of evil—as purely based on divine prohibition—challenge you to trust His wisdom even when your moral intuition screams otherwise? Will you now consciously submit your judgments to His absolute sovereignty?
?
The example of the slave committing adultery is deliberately provocative. Does it unsettle you that God could allow such evil without Himself being evil? What does your reaction reveal about your attachment to human moral categories? Are you willing to let God define good and evil for you, even when it feels uncomfortable?
?
If evil is simply what God prohibits, then your own sense of right and wrong is entirely dependent on His commands. Do you live as if that is true—or do you secretly hold God to a standard of justice you invented? How can you begin to align your conscience with the reality that He is the sole source of moral value?

The Mu'tazila and Dualist Principles on Good and Evil
Critique of Mu'tazila and Dualist doctrines on evil and benefit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila and Dualist principles that equate the witnessed and unseen and tie wisdom to benefit, and how the Mu'tazila adopted these from the Dualists.

Core Idea
Mu'tazila and Dualists both wrongly apply human moral standards to God.

The passage now critiques the Mu'tazila for equating the witnessed and unseen and for making wisdom contingent on benefit. It reveals that the Mu'tazila borrowed these ideas from the Dualists, with some modifications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on Mu'tazila and Dualists
Focus on the distinction between shahid and gha'ib and how the Mu'tazila ignore it.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila position
The Mu'tazila claim that what is evil in the witnessed is evil in the unseen, and that wisdom requires benefit.
3
Trace the Dualist influence
The author states that the Mu'tazila learned these ideas from the Dualists (Thunawiyya), who had two principles: bringing evil into existence is evil, and an act devoid of benefit is folly.
Notice the Dualist claim that any act without benefit to the agent is evil.
4
Note the disagreement on foul substances
Dualists argue that foul bodies are evil and so require another creator; Mu'tazila deny their evil and claim evil only in actions.
Both groups rely on analogy from the witnessed world.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول متبرعين في ذلك على الخصوم وفاتحين طريق العلم على المسترشدين، وهو أن القبيح والسفه عند الجمهور من متكلمي أهل الحديث هو ما ورد عنه النهي ولا نهي ولا حد على الله تعالى فلا يكون فيما يفعل مرتكبًا نهيًا ولا مجاوزا حدًا حد له ولا رسماً رسم له، فلم يكن فيما يفعل سفيها ولا فعله قبيحًا، ولهذا لم يكن تركه عبده يزني بأمته مع القدرة على المنع وتخليقه في الآلة الشدة والقوة علمه أنه مع يزني بأمته قبيحًا ولا سفها، وإن كان ذلك في الشاهد قبيحًا لكون الشاهد تحت أمر خالقه ونهيه واستحالة ذلك على الله تعالى. وبهذا يفرقون بين الشاهد والغائب، ويأبون تسوية المعتزلة بين الشاهد والغائب وتعليقهم وجه الحكمة على النفع إذ من مذهب المعتزلة أن ما قبح في الشاهد يقبح في الغائب من غير نظر إلى المعنى ويقولون: لابد لكون الفعل حكمة من نفع يوجد فيه إما للفاعل وإما لغيره، وما خلا عن النفع فهو سفه، ويتعلقون أن في الشاهد هكذا. وإنما تلقنوا هذا من إخوانهم الثنوية فبنوا المذهب على قواعدهم ونسجوا على منوالهم، فزعموا أن إيجاد القبيح قبيح وأن ما خلا عن النفع فهو سفه وتعلقوا بالشاهد، غير أن الثنوية يزعمون أن ما لا منفعة فيه للفاعل فهو قبيح، ولهذا زعموا أن كل فعل للنور فهو في نفع نفسه هو وخلاصه عن وثائق الظلمة، فصوبت المعتزلة الثنوية في هاتين القاعدتين وزعمت أن إيجاد القبيح قبيح وهو سفه، غير أنهم خالفوا الثنوية في تخليق الأجسام المستخبثة المستقذرة والأعيان الضارة، فزعمت الثنوية أنها لما كانت قبيحة كان إيجادها قبيحا وهي محدثة فلابد لها من صانع آخر، وأنكرت المعتزلة قبحها مع شهادة الله تعالى بخبثها وتشبيه الكفر الخبيث بها بقوله: ﴿ وَمَثَلُ كَلِمَةٍ خَبِيثَةٍ ﴾ وزعمت أن القردة والخنازير وإبليس - عليهم اللعنة - ومردة الشياطين كلهم أشياء مستحسنة لا خبث فيها ولا قبح، وإنما القبح في الأفعال، فأنكروا أن يكون الله تعالى خالقًا لها، ولهذا أنكروا حرمة الأعيان النازلة من حرمة الأفعال منزلة الحفظ من الحماية لما لا حرمة إلا بوصف القبح ولا قبح في الأعيان لما أنها لو كانت قبيحة لم يكن إيجادها حكمة، وكذا خالفوا الثنوية في تعليق الحكمة بنفع الفاعل نفسه بل علقوها بمطلق النفع إما للفاعل وإما لغيره، وتمسك الفريقان - أعني المعتزلة والثنوية - في الأمرين جميعًا أعني - أن إيجاد القبيح قبيح وأن الفعل الخالي عن النفع سفه – بالشاهد، فنوقضت المعتزلة بما بينا أن الباري يرى عبده يزني بأمته ويخلى بينهما مع القدرة
Then we say, volunteering in that against the opponents and opening the way of knowledge to the seekers of guidance, that evil and folly according to the majority of the theologians of the People of Hadith is that concerning which there is prohibition and no prohibition, and no limit upon God, so He is not in what He does committing a prohibition nor exceeding a limit set for Him nor a boundary drawn for Him. Thus He is not foolish in what He does, nor is His act evil. Because of this, it is not considered evil or folly that He leaves His slave to commit adultery with his slave-girl, despite His ability to prevent him and His creation in him of the instrument of severity and strength, though He knows that he will commit adultery with his slave-girl, even if that would be evil in the witnessed world because the witnessed is under the command and prohibition of its Creator, and that is impossible for God. And by this they distinguish between the witnessed and the unseen, and they reject the Mu'tazila's equation of the witnessed and the unseen and their attachment of the aspect of wisdom to benefit, since it is the doctrine of the Mu'tazila that what is evil in the witnessed is evil in the unseen without regard to the meaning, and they say: For an act to be wisdom, there must be a benefit found in it either for the agent or for another, and whatever is devoid of benefit is folly, and they cling to that being the case in the witnessed. And they only learned this from their brothers the Dualists, so they built the doctrine on their principles and wove on their loom. They claimed that bringing evil into existence is evil and that what is devoid of benefit is folly, and they clung to the witnessed. However, the Dualists claim that what has no benefit for the agent is evil, and for this reason they claim that every act of light is for its own benefit and its deliverance from the bonds of darkness. So the Mu'tazila approved the Dualists in these two principles and claimed that bringing evil into existence is evil and it is folly, except that they disagreed with the Dualists concerning the creation of foul and filthy bodies and harmful substances. The Dualists claimed that since they are evil, their creation is evil, and they are originated, so they must have another creator. The Mu'tazila denied their evil despite God's testimony to their foulness and His likening of foul unbelief to them in His saying: 'And the example of a foul word...' and they claimed that apes, pigs, Iblis (may curse be upon them), and the rebellious devils are all commendable things with no foulness or evil in them, and that evil is only in actions. So they denied that God created them, and for this reason they denied the prohibition of the essences that descend from the prohibition of actions in the manner of protection from immunity, since there is no prohibition except by the attribute of evil, and there is no evil in essences because if they were evil, their creation would not be wisdom. Likewise, they disagreed with the Dualists in attaching wisdom to the benefit of the agent himself, but rather they attached it to absolute benefit, either for the agent or for another. And both groups—I mean the Mu'tazila and the Dualists—clung in both matters, i.e., that bringing evil into existence is evil and that an act devoid of benefit is folly, to the witnessed. So the Mu'tazila were refuted by what we have shown: that the Creator sees His slave commit adultery with his slave-girl and leaves them both while having the power
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage is a combined definition, refutation, and historical analysis. It begins by stating the definition of evil (qabīḥ) and folly (safah) according to the majority of the theologians of Ahl al-Hadith: evil and folly are what is prohibited (or what is subject to a prohibition? The text has 'النهي ولا نهي' which is unclear but likely means 'prohibition and no prohibition'—perhaps a corruption). The key point is that God is not subject to any command or prohibition, so He cannot commit evil or folly. The author gives an example: if God allows His slave to commit adultery with his slave-girl despite having the power to prevent it, that is not evil in God's case, even though it would be evil in the human context because humans are under divine command. This establishes the distinction between the witnessed (shāhid) and the unseen (ghā'ib): the Mu'tazila wrongly equate them and attach wisdom to benefit. The author then traces the Mu'tazila's error to the Dualists (Thunawiyya), who claimed that bringing evil into existence is evil and that an act devoid of benefit is folly. The Dualists held that any act without benefit to the agent is evil, leading them to posit a second creator for evil things. The Mu'tazila accepted these two principles but disagreed with the Dualists about the foulness of certain substances: they denied that God creates foul things like apes, pigs, and Iblis, claiming these are actually commendable and that evil is only in actions. They also disagreed with the Dualists' restriction of benefit to the agent, extending it to benefit for others. Both groups, however, rely on analogy from the witnessed world. The passage ends with a refutation: the Mu'tazila are refuted by the example of God leaving a slave to commit adultery—He could prevent it but does not, yet it is not evil because He is not subject to prohibition.

قبيح
سفه
شاهد
غائب
ثنوية
معتزلة
حكمة
نفع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Mu'tazila doctrine on good and evil is original and independent.
Correction: The passage states that Mu'tazila learned these principles from the Dualists.

Checkpoint
What is one of the two principles that the Mu'tazila adopted from the Dualists?
A) That bringing evil into existence is evil.
B) That God is the creator of all things.
C) That the witnessed and unseen are different.
D) That benefit is not necessary for wisdom.

Key Takeaway
Mu'tazila and Dualists both erroneously apply human moral standards to God, differing only on details like the scope of benefit and the evilness of substances.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter suffering or apparent evil in the world, do you instinctively feel that God's action must have some benefit for you or for others? How might that feeling reveal an unconscious Mu'tazilite assumption? What would it take for you to trust God's wisdom even when you see no benefit?
?
The author traces the Mu'tazila error to Dualist influence. Are there any ideas you hold about God that might have come from non-Islamic sources—your culture, your upbringing, or your own reasoning? How can you intentionally submit your understanding to revelation alone, even when it feels counterintuitive?
?
The Mu'tazila denied that apes, pigs, and Iblis are evil in themselves, despite God calling them foul. Are there things that God has clearly declared evil in His Book that you hesitate to label as evil because they seem normal or beneficial to you? What does your hesitation reveal about who you trust more: your own moral sense or God's judgment?

Refutation of the Mu'tazila Using the Example of the Adulterous Slave
Refutation of Mu'tazila by the example of the adulterous slave.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the example of God leaving a slave to commit adultery refutes the Mu'tazila principle that bringing evil into existence is evil.

Core Idea
God's non-prevention of evil does not make Him evil.

The passage concludes by directly refuting the Mu'tazila using the very example introduced earlier. Since God does not prevent a known evil yet is not considered evil, the Mu'tazila principle collapses.

Guided Path
1
Recall the example from Lesson 1
Remember the example where God leaves His slave to commit adultery despite having the power to prevent it.
2
Read the refutation sentence
The passage states that the Mu'tazila are refuted by this example.
3
Understand the logic of the refutation
If the Mu'tazila claim that bringing evil into existence is evil, then God's act of not preventing the adultery (which allows evil) would be evil. But since God is not subject to moral judgment, their principle fails.
4
Apply the lesson
Recognize that this refutation underscores the transcendence of God and the error of applying human moral categories to Him.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That if God does not prevent evil, He must be evil.
Correction: No, because God is not subject to moral obligation; He is the sovereign.

According to the refutation, why is God not considered evil for allowing the slave to commit adultery?
A) Because God benefits from it.

?

?

?
If God's non-prevention of evil does not make Him evil, then why do you still demand that He must explain every trial you face? Will you surrender your judgment to His?

Divine Attributes
beginner
6 min
Wisdoms in God's Creation of Disbelief and Sin
Lists three wisdoms behind God's creation of disbelief and sin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three rational wisdoms behind God's creation of disbelief and sin, and how they affirm His perfect power, independence, and essential might.

Core Idea
God's creation of opposites displays His perfect power and independence.

A common question about divine decree is why a good God creates evil and disbelief. This passage from Imam al-Nasafi answers with divine wisdoms. The author shows that these wisdoms are not trivial but profound, serving as signs of Allah's perfect power and absolute independence. The following passage enumerates three such wisdoms, each reinforcing the transcendence and self-sufficiency of the Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once to get a sense of its rhythm. Then read the translation to understand the literal meaning.
The passage ends mid-sentence in the source; treat it as a complete listing for our lesson.
2
Identify the three wisdoms
The passage explicitly lists three wisdoms (منها). Find each one and note how they are introduced and concluded.
The first wisdom contains an additional sub-point about distinguishing eternal and temporal power.
3
Understand the first wisdom in depth
Focus on how creating opposites (good/ugly, beneficial/harmful) demonstrates perfect power. Contrast with limited power that can only produce one type.
Also note the extra point: this shows God's power over what is action of another, distinguishing His power and will from created ones.
4
Reflect on the second and third wisdoms
The second wisdom shows God's freedom from need; the third shows His independence and might, not dependent on followers or weakened by opposers.
This refutes the misconception that God's creation of evil stems from weakness or need.
5
Connect to the broader creed
These wisdoms affirm divine attributes like power, will, richness (ghani), and might (aziz). They also address the problem of evil from an Islamic theological perspective.
The incomplete third wisdom still conveys the core idea.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحكمة في خلق الكفر والمعاصي ثم نقول: إن الله تعالى في تخليق الكفر والمعصية حكما لا يحيط بها الإحصاء :منها أن يتخلق ما حسن وقبح من الأفعال ليستدل على كمال قدرته ونفاذ مشيئته حيث قدر على تخليق المتضادين وإيجاد المتقابلين، وهذا آية كمال القدرة إذ من يوجد منه نوع واحد لا غير كان مضطرًا في ذلك، وبهذا كان خلق ما حسن من الأجسام وقبح وطاب وخبث ونفع وضر وآلم وألذ حكمة بالغة وتدبيرا صائبا فكذا هذا، وفي هذا زيادة أمر وهو إظهار القدرة على ما هو فعل غيره، وبه تمتاز القدرة القديمة من القدرة الحادثة والمشيئة الشاملة من المشيئة القاصرة، فيظهر بذلك أنه تعالى قادر على محل قدرة غيره متصرف في مقدور عباده مستبد بتحصيل مراده وغير مفتقر إليه محتاج إلى إعانته وهو الغني الحميد. ومنها : أنه تعالى بتخليقه الأفعال خيرها وشرها حسنها وقبحها تبين أنه ما يفعل لا يفعل عن حاجة ولا لجلب نفع أو لدفع مضرة؛ إذ من ذلك فعله لا يفعل إلا ينتفع به. ومنها: أنه بذلك يظهر أنه تعالى غني عن خلقه عزيز بذاته لا يتعزز بكثرة أوليائه وأتباعه ولا يتقوى بأعوانه وأنصاره، ولا يضعف بتكاتف أحزاب ما
The wisdom in creating disbelief and sins. Then we say: God, exalted, in the creation of disbelief and disobedience has wisdoms that enumeration cannot encompass. Among them: that He creates good and ugly actions so that His perfect power and penetrating will may be inferred, since He is able to create opposites and bring about contraries. This is a sign of perfect power, for one from whom only one type issues is constrained in that. And by this, the creation of good and ugly bodies, and pleasant and foul, and beneficial and harmful, and painful and pleasant, was profound wisdom and sound management. So is this. In this there is an additional matter: the manifestation of power over what is the action of another. By this, the eternal power is distinguished from temporal power, and the comprehensive will from the limited will. Thus it appears that He, exalted, is powerful over the locus of another's power, is dispossessor in the measure of His servants, absolute in achieving His desire, not in need of them, needing their assistance – and He is the Rich, the Praiseworthy. And among them: that He, exalted, by creating actions – their good and evil, their beautiful and foul – shows that what He does, He does not do out of need or to attract benefit or repel harm. From that, His action is not except that He benefits by it. And among them: that by this, it appears that He, exalted, is independent of His creation, mighty in His essence, not made mighty by the multitude of His loyalists and followers, nor strengthened by His helpers and supporters, nor weakened by the coalition of any parties.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage is a listing of wisdoms (حكم) behind God's creation of disbelief and sin. The author begins by stating that these wisdoms are countless, then enumerates three. The first wisdom: God creates both good and ugly actions so that His perfect power and comprehensive will may be inferred. The ability to create opposites (like good and evil, benefit and harm) is a sign of complete power; one who can only produce one type is constrained. This is analogous to creating opposites in physical bodies. An additional point within this wisdom is that it shows God's power over what is the act of another (i.e., human actions), thereby distinguishing eternal power from temporal power and the comprehensive divine will from limited human will. This demonstrates that God is powerful over even the locus of another's power and is completely independent. The second wisdom: by creating both good and evil actions, God shows that He does not act out of need or for benefit; rather, He is the one who benefits from His own actions (i.e., He is self-sufficient). The third wisdom (incomplete): by this, it appears that God is independent of His creation, mighty in essence, not strengthened by followers nor weakened by opposition.

Key Terms
تخليق
كفر
معصية
قدرة
مشيئة
غني
عزيز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's creation of evil implies weakness or need on His part.
Correction: The passage shows that creating opposites demonstrates perfect power, not weakness, and that God's actions are not driven by need but by wisdom. He is independent and mighty in essence.

Checkpoint
What is the first wisdom mentioned for God's creation of disbelief and sin?
A) To test human obedience.
B) To manifest His perfect power over opposites.
C) To punish sinners.
D) To create variety in the world.

Key Takeaway
God's creation of disbelief and sin serves profound wisdoms: showcasing His perfect power over opposites, demonstrating His freedom from need, and affirming His absolute independence and essential might.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The passage says God's power encompasses your own actions. Do you live as if your choices are entirely under His control? How would your fear of sin and hope in His mercy change if you fully embraced this truth?
